RATING: 10
Wow! What a great game!. I love games with technology trees (or pyramids in this case), battle, and multiple ways to win. This does it all for me. My kids and I have had some great times since Christmas battling it out, or racing up the tech pyramid or culture track or pursuing the coin strategy. 

Combat is easy yet requires some strategy. It's nice not to have to roll a dice to win a battle.

Twilight Imperium III is my all time favorite because of its space theme but this game is a close second and it will see the game table more often because it is playable in 3 hours instead of the 7 hours that TI III takes.

This game has it all!

RATING: 7.5
Although this one has the full FFG standard problems - everything is a combination of tiny Euro-sized cards sprawling all over the table which inevitably slows the game to a crawl in the final rounds - and I'm certain the wheels come off with some combination of cards (FFG is famous for this flaw). And yet for all that, it completely captures the feel of a computer Civ game from Sid himself. It needs this type of treatment. And it is aptly named for sure. It is much better than Clash of Cultures by a wide margin and more epic feeling than Nations could ever be. So it's fantastic. I really liked my play of this one. I think the combat system is odd and certainly over done - it would have improved the game to simplify that part. Still, if you want the *full* Sid Civ experience in a board game, this one is the best I've seen so far.

RATING: 8.6
Esta bastante conseguido y con mucha variedad de opciones que hacer y de tipos de victoria con las que ganar. Sólo para jugones auténticos y amantes del juego de PC. Te vas a las 3-4 horas de juego, o más, pero se disfruta si te gusta de verdad. Las 2 expansiónes aportan más líderes, alguna cosilla interesante, nada determinante, pero están a precio de 2 juegos nuevos, cosa que me parece pasarse mucho por parte de Edge. Jugado a 5 con ambas expansiones, equilibran algo más parte cultural y económica y la segunda expansión mejora muy ligeramente el combate pero no hay nada decisivo, son más variedad, alguna incluso un tanto desequilibrante. En el juego de pc vas un poco a todo o puedes especializarte en algo, aquí o te especializas del principio casi en una única victoria o lo tienes complicado, y se convierte el juego en una especie de carrera hacia ese tipo de victoria... por lo que me gustaría un sistema en que una vez que se "cierre la partida por algún tipo de victoria" se hiciera un recuento de puntos de victoria de todas las categorías, que de normal seguiría ganando quien cerró partida... o puede que no... y así fomentar el uso de todo, y no dejarte la cultura casi a 0 si te centras en lo militar, etc.

Peguitas de pequeñas erratas en cartas, y de diseño de traseras de cartas de unidades de combate, que deberían ser simétricas para por si acaso, no dar pistas en combate al contrario...

Lo mejor, en mi opinión, las tecnologías y sus variables.

Lo peor del juego, sin duda es su sistema de combate, es absurdo, lineal, sin ningún tipo de emoción, muy aburrido. 

Por ello he testeado varias opciones, con las propias cartas del juego, variantes mías de reglas de combate, que, con los mismos componentes del juego, sin usar dados, o usando hasta 7D6, mejoran, equilibran y hacen muchísimo más emocionante el combate, sin apenas alargar el juego :)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Comparing the board game with the computer game makes no justice to either of the games.

Civilization the Board Game is a great game offering lot of fun. It really captures the feel of building your own nation. However, it suffers from a few annoying characteristics that I reckon to be intentional for gameplay purposes. 

First of all, technological win is very hard to achieve. Researching is more like a timer forcing the game to end at some point. Basically everyone is researching a new tech each round. This leads to a situation in which the player starting the 15th round will win, provided that he has the trade needed. You can also do very little to rush your technological progress. All this makes the players to pursue victory via warfare, culture or coins (which have nothing to do with economy).

Secondly, the culture scoring and coin mechanisms have a very artificial feel to them. Your great cities do not automatically produce culture despite having all the wonders, universities, cathedrals etc. Instead, you have to skip your whole production to get that culture out of them. Doesn't really make sense to me. The thing with the coins is that I do not really understand what do they represent. You can find them on map or get them from a tech, great person, after winning a battle, etc. or by spending trade (=money). Yes, you spend money to get coins.

Third annoying thing are the wonders. If there are no wonders worth building and no one is building them, the game sees no wonders built at all. The wonders available should be refreshed every now and then.

But if you're ready to accept the shortcomings mentioned above, you'll get a great game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.65
I like that many paths lead to victory which ensures variety, however, some paths are more difficult to play, 
especially the coins path is quite easy, but it is fixed in the first expansion. 
It is still a 4x game, the military path is too destructive for my taste. 
Play time is on the long side, there is some downtime but players may sometimes play simultaneous. 
Overall, the rules feel very overwhelming in the first game. There are a lot of cards, components, and game mechanics to keep in mind.
You have to count your generated resources each round which I do not like, it is cumbersome.
After playing the first game, rules are okay, still fiddly - especially the combat rules are just complicated.
You also have to frequently look what other players do, otherwise they might just win in the next minute. This is quite tricky, because you have to look closely from a distance due to the big game board (a lot of table space is required). Thus, the game might feel quite solitaire, if players concentrate too much on their own player boards.
I highly recommend using the condensed rules from Esoteric Order of Gamers.
On the positive side, each game creates a unique and fun story to talk about after the game is over.

Player: 2-4 (Best: 4)
Time: 120-240m

RATING: 7
A thoughtfully designed game that plays surprisingly easily given the huge number of components. It does a great job capturing many components of the computer game, especially the fun of exploration and discovery, the feeling of progress, and the manifold paths to victory.

Unfortunately, it does not replicate the computer game's sweeping scale--players are limited to only three cities, which almost always all get founded in the first few turns. The game is paced very fast--it's a rare game that lasts more than thirteen or so turns--so expect something much more frenetic than the slow and steady civilizational progress of the original game.

There are severe balance issues in the original. The economic victory is by far the easiest of the four, and the culture victory significantly more difficult. This can be pretty easily balanced with some houserules. (I recommend removing one or two trade from barracks and capping all techs that grant coins at three. It's also a good idea to increase the number of coins required for economic victory to sixteen and knock a space or two off the culture track.)

My other major complaint with the original is a rather clunky combat system, which I understand is replaced in one of the expansions. At least the bigger, better equipped army usually wins, so that's something.

On the whole, this is an enjoyable experience. The ending is usually quite dramatic as all the civs close in on their different victory conditions at about the same time and employ every trick in the book to inch ahead.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
You might want to treat my rating with caution.  Not only is it based on a single play but it was a play that made use of both expansions.

I like this Civilization more than I should.  At heart it's a game of making the optimum use of available resources to build an engine that will deliver the military power, cultural advancement or wealth necessary for the win. In this respect it is as much an efficiency game as any cube pushing euro. 

There also isn't a great deal of interaction until a likely series of endgame battles.  This Civ lacks the trading economy that was such a feature of Francis Tresham's classic design.  Add to this that despite the different victory conditions to aim for, falling behind in military technology will very probably be punished severely.  There is also no tech tree.  Given two first level techs of any kinds, I can buy any second level tech.  Horse riding and animal husbandry really can be the precursors of the printing press.  Both cultural advancement (which grants take that cards) and a combat system more reminiscent of Battleline/Schotten Totten than it ought to be, are weak.     

This game (certainly with both expansions) has a pronounced learning curve.  As with all complex efficiency engines, it will take a while to find the keys and work out what the many levers do. 

Good points there are.  These include surprisingly little downtime; turns are divided into five phases, each with a small number of options.  Despite the incongruities noted above, there is a real sense of building a civilization. I guess the beauty of there being no tech tree is that a wide range of options for advancement is always open. The various cultures also seem to be interestingly differentiated on first acquaintance. For a game of Kevin Wilson/FFG parentage that features the usual piles of cards and chits, SMC:TBG is surprisingly streamlined and lacking in fiddliness.

While this game will disappoint those who crave a box brim full of miniatures, it is up to FFG's usual good standards production-wise.  The artwork is very pleasing too.

RATING: 7
This civilization game certainly has a lot of interesting details and paths to pursue towards meeting one of the four ways to win the game: conquer a capital city, max out on the culture track, max out on the money track, or build up your tech tree and reach space flight.  It doesn't particularly flow in a euro game sense, but then again, how could it when it aspires to include essentially everything!?

It is interesting to consider this game alongside the other omnibus civ game: Clash of Cultures.  This game feels perhaps a bit more complete and dare I say educational in a good way, while C of C feels more playable.  C of C perhaps has the advantage of providing many (perhaps too many) benchmark objectives that must be met in order to win the game.  With a very nicely implemented tech tree, for my tastes, C of C is probably the better game overall.  However, this game feels like a fuller more complete exploration of civilization.  It teaches you more!  You get a serious civilization building experience.

RATING: 5
8/15/2015: This is a sandbox type game.  I can see why it's not more popular and not discussed more widely.  There are so many dials, that you need to play several times to figure out what's going on. You're going to be frustrated the first several times that you play with more experienced players.  It's a longer game, so you're going to have to invest a fair amount of time understanding the game, and I'm not sure that the payoff is worth it.  Also if you're inexperienced, it seems that luck and randomness play a large factor, which is not necessarily good in a long game like this.

That said, it is a faithful adaptation of the computer game.

The game that I'd rather play is "Through the Ages."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A 2010-es esseniek közül ez is benne volt az általam legjobban várt 3 játékban, sajnos csak a 2011-es esseni áradat közepette, 1 éves késéssel sikerült kipróbálni. 1992-94 közt sokat játszottam a PC-s változat legelső részével, és a későbbi részek többségét is próbáltam, ennek megfelelően ez nekem egy kötelező darab volt. Röviden a véleményem: jól megcsinálták, helyenként egész jól visszaadja a számítógépes változat lényegét! Szép, igényes, szinte minden oké vele...
Egyetlen nagy hibája, hogy az egyik nyerési mód teljesen esélytelen a másik hárommal szemben. A gazdasági győzelem talán a legkönnyebb, a katonai és a tudományos sem tűnik nehéznek. Ezzel szemben a kulturális győzelem szinte teljesen esélytelennek tűnik! A mi 2 személyes partinkban az egyik fél a kulturális győzelemre ment rá, ami kb. a 20. körben valósulhatott volna meg. Eközben a másik fél a 12. körben nyert gazdaságilag de úgy, hogy a katonai vagy tudományos győzelmet is választhatta volna 1 vagy 2 körön belül. Egy parti alapján talán korainak tűnik ezt kerek perec kijelenteni, de mások is írták, hogy a kulturális veszett ügy. Azért ez elég nagy tesztelési hiba (vagy mi játszottuk alapvetően rosszul).
De ettől függetlenül kifejezetten jó játék, amelynek elég bonyolult a rendszere, az a minden mindennel összefügg kategória, de a szabály végére már átlátható, és érezhető, hogy jól felépített rendszerrel van dolgunk.
A 3 szerintem ténylegesen nyerési esélyt kínáló győzelmi mód nagyon szellemes, lényegesen eltérő, és elég sok újrajátszhatóságot rejt magában szerintem.
Amit külön kiemelnék, az a csaták megoldása (külön egységkártya paklikkal, Schotten-tottenre vagy az 1960 vitáira emlékeztető módon, csak itt 1-1 kártya lehet max. egy oldalon): ötletes, élvezetes, de kicsit nagyobb erőkülönbségnél már szinte teljesen esélytelennek érzem a gyengébb felet.
Ami még említést érdemel, az a játék hossza: fél óra felpakolás, 1 óra szabálymagyarázat, és szerintem játékosonként 80-90 perces játékidő... Szóval nem rövid! A szabály egyébként elég logikus...
A játék nagyon nyelvfüggő, szóval jó, hogy kiadták magyarul!
Civilizációs játékokat kedvelőknek kötelező, de a TTA azért jobb!
(Még egy dolog: csodákat mi haszontalannak éreztük a játékban, mire építeni tudtunk volna, már majdnem vége volt a játéknak....és sokkal kevesebbet hoznak, mint amibe kerülnek... Vagy rosszul gondolom?)
---
Emlékeztem, hogy tetszett, de mivel nem akartam a 4 és fél éve játszott egyetlen partira alapozni, újra elővettem, hogy eldöntsem, eladom-e. Nos a játék szerintem nagyon jó, és néhány dolog, amit elsőre véltünk a játékról, így a második parti után teljesen más megvilágításba került. Először is a csodák nagyon is jók tudnak lenni, és megérhetik az árukat. Mi most ketten megépítettünk 7 vagy 8 csodát a 12-ből, mindketten le is cseréltünk egyet. Valószínűleg azért érte meg, mert tovább tartott a játék, mint a múltkor, ennek oka pedig az volt, hogy most jól játszottuk az aranyszerzős részt. Mert én nagyon úgy emlékszem, hogy anno úgy játszottuk, hogy a térképen a külvárosban lévő aranyat választhattam körről körre a városirányítási fázisban, ami így utólag teljesen logikátlan. Szóval az csak fix 1 arany, nem pedig jövedelem. Így viszont (jól játszva) egyáltalán nem könnyebb a 15 arany se, mint a többi cél. Ami durva, hogy ma épp az első parti után lesajnált kulturális győzelmet szereztem meg. A vége felé 1 körben 20 feletti kultúra zsetont tudtam szerezni (egyik városból pl a telepes által hazaküldött 2-vel együtt 9-et!, csodákból kör elején 4, stb.), pedig nem is azzal a néppel voltam, ami segíti a kultúrát. Hubi hasonlóan jól állt technikában, pedig nem az erre optimalizált néppel volt. Érdekesség még a mai partiban, hogy a fővárosomhoz a végén már 5 arany tartozott (jól húztam a híres embereket). Szerintem nagyon sok újrajátszhatóság van a játékban, máris elkezdtek érdekelni a kiegek, pedig tudom, hogy a hossza miatt sajnos nehezen kerül elő... A TTA a civilizációs játékok csúcsa, de ez is szuper, bizonyos dolgokban még jobb is, pl. van térkép, kézzel fogható egységek... Szóval tuti megtartom!

RATING: 9.1
Resembles the computer game a lot. WOW factor is max. The battle system is actually one of the strong elements in the game. My expectations for a pure 10 were not met, since I did not enjoy excellence in the pure civ aspect -the game is a bit more ameritrash than I'd like. Military conflict plays an important role and player interaction is more fierce than I would like. Resources are very scarce. I'd like to see a cuba-like mechanism that transforms resources into vps but there isn't any-well, there are some but you have to sacrifice your action to collect a resource. That's my greatest disappointment. Another one is that lots of the technologies -at least half I think- are aimed towards military confict and not civ development. One should try to keep a defense army and attack at the correct moment when he needs the benefits most. Wish it was more of a civ and less of an Ameritrash. Still it is a game I compare to and enjoy more than Twilight Imperium, which means: excellent work Kevin!

RATING: 6.5
This game does a great job in reproducing the spirit of the videogame, and is also a very good game: there's multiple ways to win, tech trees, combat and lots of player interaction.
Every aspect of the game is interwoven with others, and every move has lots of cascading effects.
There are some lacking aspects, however: the production is good but not great, it takes lots of space for playing (and the small components are not easily recognizable from the other side of the table), and the rulebook is typical FFGesque: lots of unanswered question and an entire paragraph missing (the one about wonders display refresh)!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Man, do I love this game!

It offers immense complexity, variety, great stories, plenty of strategy and planning. You'll be evolving your civilization through research, exploration, war, trade, and construction! It's up to you what you will focus on.

I don't know how they manage to do this but I have not played a single game of Civilization that didn't end in a nail-bitingly dramatic finale. Every game feels unique and it's always tough to say who will win the game in the end. 

The four different victory conditions make room for uncertainty, as you never quite know what victory condition the other players are going for. The various civilizations have very distinct strengths, and usually the player who uses their civilization's powers most effectively ends up as the victor.

Every decision I make feels important in this game. When I lose it is mostly because I messed up, not because of some random events that I couldn't have predicted or avoided. 

It's not for everyone though, as it is very complex. For people who primarily enjoy light games there will be too many choices to make. Also you need at least one game to get a hang of all the different strategies that are possible. There are sooo many options and decisions to make every single turn. Many people will dislike it due to analysis paralysis. Also it is very long, especially the first few games. 

That being said, if you're looking for a game that tells a different story each time you play it and that gives you a feeling of impact and control over your empire, then give this game a try!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I don’t consider myself a civilization games enthusiast but usually I feel somewhat compelled to play them whenever I’m given the opportunity: so I couldn’t draw back from trying out Sid Meier’s Civilization, which I liked much better than I thought I would.

Sure, there are many things in Sid Meier’s Civilization I still can’t stand, such as the limited culture cards hand, the small number of cities you may build and the dedicated single resource they may collect each turn, yet all in all I enjoy this game a lot, as it really gives you the feeling you’re building your own civilization: there are just so many things you can do and so many ways to win that in the end you can’t be disappointed by your playing it.

Nonetheless, all this complexity comes with a downside: the game is so exacting you don’t usually care for what your opponents are doing in their turns as you have to carefully plan your moves in advance, which makes interaction among the players minimal and the whole playing like a huge brain-burner you simply have to optimize.

But I guess this is why Civilization is regarded as a heavy game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Yet another chapter in the quest for the holy grail of civilization building games unfolds. Every time I see a game like this I wonder, will this be the one? AH's "Civilization" set an early high bar. "Mare Nostrum" was close but needed an expansion to finish the design; "Rise of Empires" was worthy but the rulebook was badly flawed in production; "Tempus," was hyped but quickly  disappointed; the Eagle Game's original go at "Sid Meier's Civilization: the Board Game" tried hard but missed badly. The best board game implementation of Sid's classic PC game, so far, has been "Through the Ages." ("7 Ages"? Don't get me started).

So, when the FFG version appeared I had to pay attention. It looked and sounded good. I couldn't wait to try it.
++++++
After a couple of plays, I have mixed feelings. It's quite good, very good in fact. It has captured the feel of the PC game without being bogged down in too much micro-management ("counting cows"). There are a lot of moving parts, but after a couple of sessions the flow of the game smooths out and things fit together very well. The way the "armies" and "military units" are managed, along with the combat system, is novel and clever. 

There are several niggles however. The tech card system is a bit bloated and space consuming - it might have been better to use a paper record of some type like the original "Civilization" or "Age of Renaissance" (a handy such file is already available). I'm not yet certain that the different paths to victory (tech/gold/culture/conquest) are balanced. And, this game really needs to be played by four - three player games are often likely to be problematic for one of the players.

So, another worthy entry in the Civ family and a game I expect to be playing a fair bit; but, the quest goes on... 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Inital rating - need to play it more.

I so want to like this game, but inital feeling is it is just too long and fiddly for the streamlined experience I was hoping for. Why play this when you could play it on the PC and have all the fancy graphics and the computer do the fiddly mechanics?

Not yet convinced it really works multiplayer, if one player decides to go the military route ... But all multiplayer conflict games have this potential problem, and I could see this decend into AP hell with 4 ...

Why didn't they include a nifty dial to track the trade and production and culture values for each of your cities, game seems to end up with players counting these values over and over and over.

Final thought - why play this instead of through the ages? This is a bit easier to teach, a bit more forgiving of early mistakes (but not much) and has a map - but I think I prefer to play TtA.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Juego de desarrollo de civilización, con tablero modular, para 2 a 4 jugadores. Cada jugador empieza con una ciudad y dos fichas. A lo largo de la partida puede llegar a tener hasta tres ciudades.  Hay varios tipos de victoria: Económica (si consigues reunir 15 monedas), cultural (si llevas al final del track de cultura), militar (si conquistas la capital de otro jugador) o tecnológica (si llegas a desarrollar la única tecnología de nivel 5) que hay en el juego.

Cada turno tiene 5 fases: cambio de turno y de gobierno, comercio, gestión de la ciudad (cada ciudad te permite una acción a escoger entre tres: comprar/construir, dedicarte a las artes y otra que no me acuerdo), mover piezas y desarrollar tecnología.

Las reglas no son complicadas en absoluto y las posibilidades del juego son muy grandes. El tema está muy trabajado. Muy recomendable.. es del nivel del civilization original, pero se puede jugar en menos tiempo (a cuatro jugadores, pongamosle 4 horas).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Juego de exploración y desarrollo de civilización. Cada jugador tiene una ciudad (puede llegar a tener tres) y dispone de tantas acciones como ciudades tenga. Admite muchos caminos hacia la victoria. Ésta puede ser militar (conquistando una capital de un adversario), cultural (llegando al final del track de cultura), económica (consiguiendo 15 monedas) o tecnológica (desarrollando la unica tecnología de nivel cinco en el juego). Es un juego intenso, y con la profundidad de los juegos clásicos de civilización, pero en mucho menos tiempo (4 horas, aproximadamente, quizás menos).
Recomendable.

RATING: 7
A surprisingly big hit in my gaming group.

This game has a ton of components. Setup is long. You'll spend a lot of time adding up values--expect to spend a minute or two manually counting up trade and production every turn.

But if you can get past the clunkiness, it really does do a remarkable job translating an incredible number of features from the video game. Research, exploration, culture, trade, warfare, resources, great people, wonders, multiple victory conditions... they're all there. It's kind of spectacular. 

The last few turns tend to be really exciting as everyone closes in on a different victory condition. Every single game I've played has had a fun and dramatic finish.

I think the mechanic that limits the number of available resources and buildings is very clever and works really well. You can potentially hoard all the Iron to give yourself a military advantage, or hoard the Incense to stop another player from winning culturally. There's always a race to see who can get the Barracks, because if they split 2-2-0 in a 3-player game, the player with 0 is going to be massively vulnerable. It's good stuff.

The biggest gameplay issue is a rather clumsy combat system. This is greatly improved by the Wisdom and Warfare expansion.

Finally, here are two house rules that I think improve the base game:
--Remove the 2 trade from the Barracks. (This building is already strong enough without also being a terrific economic building.)
--Increase the economic victory requirement from 15 to 16 coins, and have each coin-granting tech max out at 3 (rather than 4) coins. (Economic victory is far too easy in the base game.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
It succeeds at being reminiscent of the computer game, and is a great game on its own.

I like the tech pyramid, the exploration on the map, and the combat system. I don't like that the different nations seem a bit unbalanced, at least to me as a newbie. The setup is a bit tedious with so many components.

I realized that I'm actually not a big fan of games of this complexity level. That's not a critique of this game in particular, others probably think that this is the best part about it: This game has so many options to explore, a huge number of technologies, many different nations, buildings, wonders and culture cards and different victory conditions. This means that even after several plays, I'm feeling like I'm still learning the game, and not really playing it. And since the game takes quite long, I just don't find the time needed to fully explore this game. Not knowing your options forces you to play by gut most of the time, instead of actually evaluating different options and developing a strategy.

RATING: 7
Short version: there's a lot here to like but it's a little too footery and a lot too long to be a game I can love - I do like it though.  Long version: this has most of the things you expect from a Civ game - cities which gather resources ... which let you research technologies ... which unlock buildings ... which enhance your cities ... and an exploration/combat element as you send your scouts and armies into the unexplored wilderness to discover friendly villages, vanquish hostile tribes, establish new cities and conquer other empires.  There are, of course, Wonders to build too and opportunities to improve your culture and wealth.  While this is similar to the computer game, it's done in a proper board gamey way, which mostly works well and has a 'euro' feel to it, which I like.  What I didn't like was the 'take that' elements, which are mostly crudely done, and some other randomness - the problem isn't that there's a lot of it but rather that what there is can have a big impact, without there being enough opportunities for it to even out.  The presentation is mostly impressive, very plush as you'd expect from FFG, but I found some of the icons a little unclear and some of the components could be better designed to make the game easier to play without reference to the rules - which are on the long side but not too complex - e.g. the technology cards and building counters don't show discounts on Wonders or terrain restrictions on building.  Unfortunately, even with three players, it's a bit too long to finish in an evening and the addition of a fourth (the recommended number) will delay rather than hasten the end, making it an all day affair - which is simply too long for what it is.  Shame, as I do like it.

RATING: 7
I LOVE the computer game Civilization; it is by far my most played computer game ever (Civ II and III), so I was very eager to try this game. What I found did have a few similarities to the computer game, but they were mostly shallow (and I actually think that other civilization games like Through the Ages and Clash of Cultures remind me more of PC Civ).
Even if the game shouldn't be judged on how much it reminds me of Civ, but rather be judged on its own, it's impossible to totally ignore this fact. 

Now, the game itself is good, but nothing more. I have played it a couple of times, and the expansion definitely improves the gameplay (especially the horrible combat system!), but it's still lacking that "something" which makes me want to play it several times, which has been the case with games like Nations and Clash of Cultures which I love. 
The best part of the game is probably the scoring system; I love that it is, as in Civ, is possible to win by several means, and I've seen my games end by different victories, which is definitely a plus - although some strategies seem better than others, but of course it's not possible to flawlessly balance many different victory conditions. The worst part of the game, however, is by far the combat system which I don't even want to mention as it makes me mad... It's just plain sad to see what is otherwise a decent game being ruined by a rock-paper-scissors system. Also, while it's a "good enough" game, I will just rather play Clash of Cultures or Nations if I am to play a longish civilization game.

RATING: 7
So, as far as a Kevin Wilson game goes, this one definitely feels more streamlined and all the rules seem necessary to every game.  But even still, there is not that much different here that really sets it apart from other epic games of civ building.  The tech tree is probably the best part of the game and how it works as a pyramid, with a level 2 tech only requiring two level 1 techs under it.  The other neat thing is how cities count as actions, so you want to make racing to three cities a priority above all else. This causes a problem in the early game, as how quickly you can get your second/third cities going is completely dependent on how badly the map screws you (luck that can't be mitigated) The other worthwhile mention is the four different ways to win: culture, military, economy, or tech (the level 5 space tech).  Of the four, Military seems like it would be the hardest but is needed as a threat and to add some player interaction to the game.  The Bad for this game starts off with the fighting - its way to fiddly and hard to figure out for what it is and unless you have someone to really explain it well, I can see it suffering a good bit for new players as they scratch their heads.  The game is also very long, 4 to 5 hours with another 30 minutes of rules.  You need some snappy players to keep things moving or you may start to stare around the room when its not your turn.  The game also seems like it starts much too slow and there is 0 interaction for the first 1/2 of the game, at least.  All in all, I highly recommend a game of Epic Rune Wars instead of this one for more fun for your time.  

--update: Played again and feel the same. Another observation: how fighting is implemented definitely suffers from the 'gang up on the leader' problem these games tend to have.  I don't really care for the 'laying low' strategy to win.  If you spend your time acquiring military cards (units), then when your forced to fight you lose your strength, sending you backwards from your goal (unless you can manage only 1 attack against the capital).  If you go for cultural, economic, or tech victory, your progress is never lost.  In our game, one person had to beat up on the perceived leader, forcing them to exhaust their cards - then the third player swooped in for the easy military victory.  Losing progress kinda sucks in games like this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I wanted to like this game a lot.  I wanted to give it an unbiased independent review and not fall into the trap of "it doesn't live up to the computer game for X,Y,Z reasons..."

After a few plays, it's clear that the game suffers from a few design flaws that make it just not fun.  Let's start with the good. 

Things Civilization does well: 
1) It (at least in theory) allows different paths to victory; from science, to culture, to economic, to military supremacy.  
2) The starting civs feel unique and varied
3) the tech tree is wonderful, and the pyramid deign of discovering techs that build on top of previous knowledge is a nice mechanic
4) building cities and upgrading their outskirt tiles gives you a nice feeling of becoming progressively more powerful in the game
5) wonders add powerful enhancements that are truly game-changing
6) the government system is a great addition to giving extra policy flavor to match your needs at the moment. 

Here's where Civ game design falls short
1) The different paths to victory are a distraction.  If you focus on military, you will win the game.  Even if you don't technically take over other capitals, destroying enemy troops, cities, and blockading other cities will basically ensure you have the edge to win.  This game is just a race to see who can do it first.  
2) Some starting civs have powerful bonuses that aid their military power in ways that feel overpowered.  Since military maneuvers are the easiest ways to win the game, civs that start with this type of bonus start with an advantage
3) The tech tree actually feels like it allows progress too quickly.  Tier 2 techs can come out in as little as 2 turns, and Tier 3 techs can come out after the 5th turn.  The higher tier techs are extremely powerful, especially the ones that again, favor military action. 
4) Combat outcomes are poorly designed and it feels wonky.  Instead of distinct units that roam across the map, you get army figures that represent a generic military force, and you keep a standing pool of all your military units nearby.  When you go to battle, you draw cards from your unit pool to create a hand size, this hand size may increase depending on how many figures are in your army for instance, or if you're defending from a city attack.  2 major problems with combat are that the defender can never choose to retreat or avoid risking units in combat, and that the defender is at a distinct disadvantage because they have to play first.  This has profound consequences.  Civ is an attacker friendly game, where someone with a military advantage can always press that with little risk of losing their units, while obliterating a defending force that cannot choose whether to involve or spare their units from combat.  The outcome is that a player who wins battles will keep winning them, especially as their opponent depletes all their available units from combat.  After a battle, the loser has to spend entire turns making up for lost units and figures (which must be purchased separately) and the winner can spend that turn further building their military advantage or making their cities better, whereas the loser can't keep up.  
5) All of this might have been fine, except Civ has one crucial design flaw: it's not a quick game, and if you're stuck at a disadvantage, there really is very little room to build, research, or fight your way out of it.  This means you can be "losing" for an hour while your opponent mops the floor with you.  2 player games are especially bad at this.

Tl;DR - I play a variant where the attacker has to play the first card in combat, and either side can choose to stop playing their unit cards at any time.  (effectively retreating to save units, but still losing the battle and the winner gets all loot, effects)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Let me start off by saying that I love Civilization the computer game. I can think of no more fun or challenging experience in computer gaming than playing Civilization. The battles are exciting, and developing your grand civilization is a rewarding way to spend an afternoon. 

The board game reminds you of how much book-keeping the computer does for you automatically. 

First is the setup. I bought the earlier incarnation of the Civilization board game from Eagle Games, so I knew that I wasn't in for a simple game. When I saw the dials for each of the Civilization cards, my heart leapt for joy. No more counting coins. Armies weren't represented by individual pieces anymore. The ranking of units looked streamlined. Everything looked much simpler and cleaned up than the Eagle Games version. 

Unfortunately, there's still a LOT of moving pieces and cards to this game. Our initial setup took about an hour to get everything sorted out. Sorting through the tech level cards was a bit of a chore because some of the colors look very similar to each other (blue and green for me). There's one space-age card that is special and needs to be kept out. Each of the improvements is double-sided, so figuring out which went to what piece on the market board was challenging the first time around. Then there's the cultural cards and markers, resource markers, government cards, wound markers... the list goes on and on of markers and such. Granted, everything has a place, and is well defined, but it takes some getting used to in order to set up quickly. 

Once the game is set up, you place your city, scout, and army, and then begin the game. Unfortunately, by the time we got to this point, I was already thinking if we had played the computer game, we would have been playing about two hours through by now. Had their not been the strong correlation between this game and the computer game of the same namesake, we probably would have overlooked this. Alas, for us the temptation to chuck this to the side and play the computer game was too great a temptation. My soldiers found a village, and after leafing through the combat rules, we gave up. 

I'm positive that the rules for this game are vastly better than the Eagle Games version of Civilization. From what I saw, it was streamlined, and potentially a fun game. Be prepared to take over 4 hours to get to the point of having fun with this game. If you have the patience to persevere, you can likely find a great game. Maybe I'm getting older, but I don't have the patience to play a game that takes this long to get to the good stuff, especially when the computer version is a few clicks of the mouse away. And the computer is so much better and faster at set-up, book-keeping, and clean-up.

RATING: 10
This is a wonderful game about civilization. The quality of components is beautiful like usual FFG standard, with some of the arts come from Civ V the PC game. It really captures the gameplay of Sid Meier Civilization (in PC), with some of it are streamlined for boardgame. There are:

- City outskirts (and resources around it)
- Buildings
- Modular map
- Tech Pyramid (brilliant idea!!)
- Wonders
- Great People
- Asymmetry player (civ) power
- Multiple winning conditions
- Some luck, but easily mitigated with good play

All of the 4x (Explore, Expand, Exploit, Exterminate) are presented here. Although it bears the same theme with Through the Ages, surprisingly it's very different. SM Civ focuses on parts where Through the Ages only touch it lightly. It's clearly a great game. 

Also, this is rare for FFG, but the base game is completely balanced! There is really no need for expansions, except for more culture cards and civilization choices (Errata and FAQ's still needed though). But that's minor.

RATING: 8
Civilization building for players who love to play loooong games. You can try and win in 4 different ways: cultural, economic, technological or military. It is possible for different players to achieve victory in the same game round. The game does not provide for any tie breaking rule. This can lead to quite some discussions. But it's not bad really that the players themselves can choose how they resolve ties, and whether or not breaking a tie is necessary at all. Why would it not be possible for several civilizations to share victory? After all, countries are better off working together instead of trying to dominate eachother. Of course, it would be nice if the rules were to stipulate the fact that players have to decide before they start how they will resolve ties.
Battles are somewhat strange due to the trump rule. Spearmen trumping tanks? Don't think so. But seeing how your civilization expands is very nice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It's difficult to teach this to anyone who's never played the videogames, but it does quite accurately create a simplified version of most of the mechanics.  My main complaint is that more playable civilizations should have been included with the base game, or that small box expansions of extra civilizations should have been released.  Including America next to Ancient Egypt and Rome is Bizarre.  America is not a civilization.  Another main complaint is that battles aren't very well executed until you add the Wisdom and Warfare expansion.  

The expansions are really almost mandatory, but integrating them requires that you remove content from the previous games which is tedious and leaves you with a stack of components that barely fit in the original box.  If this was rereleased as a single "big box" or "deluxe" version with both expansions built into a box they fit with a fancy box liner and a few bonus civilizations it'd make the top 10 list for BGG for sure.  So they should do that!  It saddens me that such a good game gets so little attention because of these minor publication oversights.

RATING: 7.5
After buying this, it was months before we actually played it; the sheer number of components, the length of the rulebook, and the scope of the game were very intimidating.

When we finally did sit down and play it though, we ended up loving this game.  Exploring, building, upgrading, amassing troops -- it's all quite fun.

As far as complaints:  Like I said, the rulebook is enormous, and could've been way trimmed down.  Also, the game is a bit on the lengthy side, even with just 2 people (though everyone takes many of the actions simultaneously, so it doesn't feel as long as it is).  Also, it really sucks that the troops you'll be deploying in battle are randomly drawn from your hand of standing forces; I would much rather assign certain troops to a specific front than rely on luck of the draw.

Overall, I really enjoy this game, even though I was sure it was going to be a chore to play. It hardly makes it to the table though, it's just so complex that it's daunting to re-learn all the rules.

RATING: 6.5
I miss the micromanagement I could do in Through the Ages.  Here, you don't manage your resources, happiness, food, etc.  Instead, you just decide which you will spend that turn.  

I enjoyed it, but I felt like there was too much waste.  What I mean is that each city can only do one action per turn.  You can use your Hammers to produce something (building, unit, Wonder, etc.) OR you can harvest (usu. only) one special good OR claim Culture.  Because of this, the game essentially rewards specialization.  I focused on Trade, so I could research technologies (I was the Russians), and never even claimed any Culture the entire game!  This waste seemed unrewarding to me.  I felt like I was on rails with my strategy.  It wouldn't have been viable for me to switch to Culture production or Coin production, for example.

I'm not sure how I feel about the combat system yet either.  I miss the elegance of TTA's.

Some more thoughts: 
I like how in TTA, I USE all the technologies I get.  In Civ, often, I felt like I was just searching for the best tech simply because I had the trade to spend.

Civ has significantly more luck:
--random draw for starting civ (see my "rails" comment above)
--random military unit draws.
--random Culture card draws.
--random Hut and Village spoils.
--random terrain exploring.

That said, I do want try it again at some point and see if my feelings change. But, it would have to be a spectacular game to make me want to play it over Through The Ages: A Story of Civilization.

Update: With Clash of Cultures out now, I have no desire to go back and try this again.

RATING: 8.5
This game has so much to offer in terms of playability and options, we didn't even manage to see everything on the first playthrough. The gameplay resembles the Civilization computer game a lot, except now you can actively discuss alliances and trades around the table, rather than in front of a screen.

The only 'downside' I've thus far noticed is that once someone is on a road to victory, the only real way to stop them is by going to war. When played with 4 players, the one who is nearing the end before the others may well face 3 armies at once. I put downside in quotation marks, since it can easily be avoided, but it's not necessarily something I'd see civilizations do. In one game I won by cultural victory, but one more turn and I would've been annihilated by the other players. That doesn't give me a fulfilling feeling, as my 'civilization' would be doomed in the end.

However, the game is great and I don't regret having purchased it!

RATING: 7
Takes a significant amount of time just to learn how to play. Learning this (along with the Fame and Fortune expansion) from the rulebook is not for the timid.

The game itself absolutely does what it sets out to do... simulate taking a civilization from modest beginnings to world dominance. There are many paths to victory and a pretty wide open world to explore and exploit.

But it's a huge amount of work to play it. There's absolutely fun to be had and strategies to ponder... but if the fun factor could be compressed into a shorter time and some of the fiddliness lessened, I'd be able to bump up my rating a bit.

EDIT: Nope, our group just can't do this one. Even though everyone likes it, the game just takes way too long. We have 5 players, and it's 6-9 hour slog for us... and twice now we've sort of rushed/conceded the ending without playing all of the last round.

Maybe we all just like to think too long about our options... I can't imagine playing this game in 3 hours with 5 players without dramatically rushing every single decision.

RATING: 2
3 player game, played for 3 hours until I literally had a meltdown and freaked out because I couldn't take it anymore.  Probably had another 4 or 5 hours to go so it was a mercy killing.

Sid Civ is clearly a labor of love.  The amount of detail is mind blowing.  Things kinda worked: exploration, tech, I didn't even mind combat, I never really understood combat but whatever.  City upgrades and Wonders were interesting.  Special dudes and culture whammies and tech powers and cards and tokens and tokens, cards, cards, tokens, tokens, tokens, more cards a few more tokens (*INHALE*) and of course, Civ specific powers.    

But here's the thing.  There are at least 4 different currencies to track, convert, spend.  By the time I quit, I had to process on at least 20 different little fucking power cards with sometimes two or three different uses each.  I imagine this number doubles or triples if you play to completion.  Never mind paying attention to the other players, win conditions, or even your own future planning.  This was pure treading water and hoping either for a quick rescue or a quicker death.

The amount of minutae fiddling, visual noise, and mental clutter demanded by this game is absolutely stupefying. 

It is almost like someone said to themselves: "I really like playing Civ on a computer but I hate the way it counts, shuffles, randomizes, moves, jostles, every little goddamn piece for me.  I want to do all that myself, manually, forever." 

Sid Civ is a game I think I would have gone bonkers about in college.  Now, cranky old person that I am, I feel that if I live a good and virtuous life, I will be very lucky and happy to never have to see or play or think about this game ever again.  

The worst gaming experience of my life.

RATING: 7
I liked the game...I think.  Definitely need a few more plays to make up my mind.  Right now, my rating could go up as much as 1 point or down as much as 2.  Is there enough strategy, or is it just a fiddily, chaotic race?  How scripted are the different nations?  How much will play time come down with repeat plays?

I taught the rules and found it to be a challenging explanation.  The rulebook is good, but there are some ambiguities.  That said, this is a complex game.  This is no euro.  In fact, I'd say it is definitely more complicated than Tresham's Avalon Hill Civilization.  And for 4 newbies, the length is very comparable to  AH's Civ.

This game is fiddly, lots of special effects on cards with weird timings.  It has a good chunk of "Surprise...take that" mechanic thrown in which can sometimes laugh in the face of strategy. Hopefully with more plays, the tech and culture cards will become more familiar and mitigate the chaos.

That said, I do like me a good Civ game!  Hope it goes up from here.

Update: Second play completed in 6.5 hours.  It's a fun game, but I'm worried about the cascading effect by a player that has some bad fortune militarily.  If one player loses a battle and a big chunk of their standing army, then the game implodes.  The weakened player's cities can be taken...thus robbing them of their few remaining military cards.  Now they have even more trouble rebuilding (buying cards) due to lack of city actions.  This leaves their capital exposed.  Ultimately it becomes a race to see who can knock out the weak player's capital.

Update #:  Had to downgrade it from 8 to 7.  Just too many moving parts. Economic strategy is too strong.  Plus expansion puts in more moving parts!  Fun game but exhasuted by the end of it due to too many seperate cards/pieces.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[ImageId=780487]

Sid Meier’s Civilization: The Board Game (SM’s Civilization: TBG) was bit of a surprise for me. I am a big fan of the computer game series, but am not normally part of Fantasy Flight Game’s target demographic (with the exception of Arkham Horror, Battlestar Galactica, and Chaos in the Old World all of which I own and enjoy), and had largely dismissed the game based on that perspective. At BGG.Con I ended up trying the game mostly at the prompting of a geekbuddy whom had been a good sport about trying the games I was interested in, so I figured the least I could do would be to give this one a go. After the game was over, I became intrigued enough by this one that we ended up playing it again the same day, and again the following day, racking up three plays over the course of the convention.

The game itself adheres to the conventions of the game, with enough streamlining that it works as a board game. Many aspects of the game have been abstracted down to a basic level or removed entirely, leaving a game that catches the essential nature of the game without taking the many, many hours that a more faithful translation would require. Wonders, a tech tree (of sorts), buildings, an initial fog-of-war clouded map, and unique civilization powers are all present, allowing for a real Civ feel.

The game’s structure itself is pretty simple. The game is divided into five straightforward phases, each of which has a small number of available actions you can take. The complexity comes from the depth of decisions that you can make within those phases. For example, during the technology phase, the only decision you make is what technology to research. That decision however is so deep that it can quickly overwhelm players who are new to the game. Early games are likely to run a bit long because of the time it will take for individuals to absorb the vast amount of options that are available, but I suspect that after this happens the game can be finished in a fairly tight 3 hours.

Further plays caused a rapid decline in my opinion of SM's Civilization: TBG. The game is an excellent adaptation of the Civilization-video game but my frustration with the sort of depth and nuance you can get out of these games has declined, resulting in a similar decline in my opinion of this style of board games. You can read more about my specific complaints here:
[blogpost=7716][/blogpost]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
Chalk up another missed opportunity at a playable civilization game. Predictably Fantasy Flight falls victem to more of their baroque-yet-inelegant design choices. Rather than inject any abstractions, FF has us literally turning dials and counting up icons turn after turn, as if players enjoy the tasks computers do in the PC version.

On the plus side, they did neatly handle the battles and also did a bang-up job on the tech tree, the most essential part of the game experience. 

As per FF orthodoxy, there are tons of tiny text on tiny cards. Also the standard few viable paths to victory (that might or might not be balanced) and a too-large-dose of unstructured negotiation. Fanboys with excess free time on their hands might rave, but for the 4+ hour playing time I'm skeptical this one is ever worth the time investment

RATING: N/A
Ieri sera partita di prova con la morosa a Sid Meier's Civilization  Shocked 
Spulzello, imbusto le carte, mi giro verso la fidanzata e: 
"Lo proviamo??" 
"Certo!" 

Le sceglie gli Egizi e io i Cinesi, il manuale lo avevo letto per due giorni circa e quindi grosso modo il più me lo ricordavo a memoria, ma nello spiegare il gioco a lei, guardare un po' cosa potevo e non potevo fare, entrambi ci siamo dimenticati di usare le caratteristiche delle nostre civiltà per tutta la partita... 

Iniziamo esplorando le varie zone limitrofe, depredando villaggi e barbari. 
Spiegato che vi sono 4 modi per vincere, nessuno dei due si è puntato su uno in particolare ma abbiamo sviluppato tutti le strade, tralasciando all'inizio quella militare. 
Verso metà partita punto forte sulla cultura e arrivo velocemente a metà, mentre lei puntava un pò più sulle tecnologie, forte dell'elevato numero di punti commercio attorno alle sue due città. 
A fine partita, rendendomi conto che non ho mai usato l'abilità dei cinesi, che mi avrebbero dato più punti cultura avrei vinto molto prima della fine della partita ma ormai... 
Quando decido di puntare un pò sul militare lei mi segue a ruota per evitare di scoprirsi, allora educatamente le faccio fuori uno scout, un grande personaggio e le distruggo due edifici con una carta. 
Lei, mascherando non troppo bene uno sguardo omicida, incassa e mi nuclearizza una città, purtroppo per me quella che mi produceva più cultura tra le mie tre a disposizione... questa mossa mi rallenta nel rush finale, facendomi arrivare ad un solo punto cultura dalla vittoria, ma ha permesso a lei di costruire la quinta tecnologia e vincere...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A decent implementation with a few niggling problems.  I like the fact that there is very little downtime in the game, other than watching other players resolve a combat.  I also like the tech pyramid concept, instead of a tech tree.   Turns go by quickly and the game keeps you engaged throughout.  I also admired that there are 4 different routes to victory and VPs aren't an element of the design at all.  It's also nice to see FFG using a higher quality plastic instead of the cheap bubblegum plastic they have been using.   My complaints are about the massive amount of book-keeping that the game requires.   You are constantly counting up small icons and adjusting dials.   That's not a good thing when going from software to boardgame.  Culture is represented in the game by increasingly powerful "take that" cards, and the theme kind of falls apart in that area - it doesn't really make sense.  I also found the text on the cards (wonders, especially) very small and impossible to read from a distance.   So, in the end, there are some "user interface" problems with this game, but at a macro level it plays pretty well and I would be willing to play it some more.

RATING: N/A
It has everything that you want in a civilization game, name it wonders, great person, culture&arts, military, nuclear, trade, production, government&politics, etc. You won't notice the time passed by as you're fully immersed in the theme/gameplay.

The tech tree is an interesting part of the game. There are four ways to win the game, one is to reach the end of the culture track, second is to advanced in the level 5 tech tree, third is by economy, and fourth is by invading another player's capital. It seems hard to advance on the culture track (except for Chinese) but the expansion fix this.

The board game implement the video game version with the same name, brilliantly.

RATING: 4
I've tried this game twice. Once with two players, and once with four.  Both games were pretty misserable.

First of all, the game is very long.  I does keep you pretty engaged, since it is a million little actions.

With two players, my opponent had the chinese and was able to just charge me and crush me with military.  Even though my cards were all better, he would never lose anything, and I would lose one.  It helped that he started with 3 - 3s and I had to upgrade my military through advancements.  I also lost a few cards to barbarians and he was crushing the barbarians.  Perhaps if everyone started with 3- 2's, it would be ok, and fix this, or if Chinese were not allowed in 2-3 players.

The 4 player version, literally turned into a kingmaker game, where the last turn one of the players got to clearly pick who he wanted to win (not himself) by either canceling an event card or not.  After 3 hours of playing, it sucked the game came down to such a small decision.

Rating this game as a 1 based one I would never play it ever again.

Edit: To be fair and honest. The expansions seem to actually address some of the glaring weaknesses of this game, but I still maintain it is just aweful out of the box.

RATING: 4
I've moved on from playing 4X games on table; prefer them as isolated experiences against a computer AI. Why they thought they could capture the magic of Civ in a boardgame is beyond me. While everything may work (I'll keep allegations of brokenness out of this comment), I didn't find it very fun. It's one of those hybrid euro-AT games that doesn't satisfy enough of either. Too long, prone to player elimination, and the combat system may sound good on paper but in practice, it's tedious. For my money, I prefer the way Through the Ages treats the entire subject.  

***

So this play ended up creating a WBC story that I'm sure I'll tell for years. I was two coins away from winning. All I had to do was switch to Feudalism and hopefully pull a Great Person with a coin from the cup and I could win this turn. Woo hoo! Except I then realized I had read "Fundamentalism" as "Feudalism". I didn't have the Feudalism tech! OMG. Well, at least I could pull a GP from the cup of GP for a coin. Where's the cup? Why can't I find it?

Time freeze. Memory flash. A sick feeling in my stomach. What's happening? Why am I remembering how I cleaned the table of debris including some cups... cups... CUPS! Oh crap, I threw out the cup that had the Great Person chits! Oh crap! Run to the bin. It's a new bag; they cleaned it out! Find maintenance; go to the trash bin. I'm outside with Doug (the owner of the game) and we're standing in the rain tearing open trash bags and digging through trash trying to find the cup. It smells. I'm getting wet. Uuuuugh. I finally convinced him that we should finish the game and I would contact FFG for replacement pieces. He agreed. Then he assassinated one of my GP's with a coin and I was out of it at that point... And I did get FFG to send him a complete replacement set of Great People.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
2011-02-22

11:34 AM

Jeebus there are a lot of chits.

—————————————————


2011-02-26

11:33 AM

I have one complaint about the components. The chits, cards, figures, etc can be divided as follows: player pieces (color-specific - cards, figures), character pieces (leader-specific - civilization card and culture track marker), and community pieces (wound markers, buildings).¹ Most but not all of the pieces fit nicely in the categories of player pieces or community pieces, but there the two leader-specific pieces do not. It would have been nice to been able to divide the pieces more simply when storing the game and I feel it could have been much simpler to learn if this distinction had been made in the rule book.


1. The resource markers *seem* like they are community pieces but a careful look at the rule book reveals that one set is placed out per player, meaning they can be placed with other player pieces.

—————————————————

Además, como tienes que robar al azar las unidades que combatirán en las batallas, has de planear bien tu ejército, tanto en lo que respecta a miniaturas como unidades. RATING: 6
Not my sort of game, but I was interested to learn it. We played as 2p and Mike knew it well enough already to give me a pasting, plus we agreed at the start not to attack cities (I'm no wargamer). I was most interested to see how it differed from the PC game, and whilst there are similarities, there is no tech tree; you need only support a higher tech with two lower, in a pyramid stack. I made various mistakes, mainly in the choosing of my techs - I didn't get irrigation until 2 turns after getting my scout to place I wanted to build my 3rd city - I left engineering late in the game so I didn't benefit from splitting to buy 2 military units. But I made a creditable stab at it and got near the end of the culture track. Mike was a few turns of getting the 5th level tech, but morphed into collecting money, so he won earlier than we reckoned.

I did like the game, but I struggle to see how you can win without ramping up your military. Mike's Egyptians got a ton of free buildings and that gave him a constant edge (he could use production on units instead), so I was never going to win. I thought there was some waste; the culture cards seemed feeble and some tech was blank/worthless or hard to gain from. I'd like to see a 2nd edition with gamer tweaks. The military battles seemed dumb, RSP is not a good tool. The board design and resource wheel were well made, and there's a lot for non-gamers to try, but this is really a game for hardened gamers and grognards.

RATING: 6
Multiplayer direct conflict in spades with all the accompanying problems.  If you consider yourself well within FFG's target market, I'd recommend it highly.  For myself, I believe I'll strongly favor only 2-player after exploring the multi-player a bit. Too much feels out of control and I feel like the game relies a little too heavily on players to play "right."  Still, it seems like quite a lot of civ in a somewhat reasonable play time.  

The main thing this game lacks in my opinion is interaction on more levels than military and random culture cards.  When I play this game, I feel like I'm simply playing my own efficiency game depending one what famous people, nation, or cards I'm dealt.  At some point you look to defending yourself militarily or putting pressure on other players, but beyond that everything feels pretty much like you're just doing your own thing.  I'd prefer a "civ" game to give me something like Innovation where cards like gunpowder screw over cards with castles, or banking screws over cards with industry.  Each aspect of a civilization has it's own strengths and weaknesses not just military.

RATING: 6.5
Another traded game. And again, not a bad game, but just not what I wanted from a Civ game. Seemed more about managing your money than it did on anything else. Honestly, it felt to tactical. I personally would think a Civ game would be a grandscale, strategic game that would last across the ages, rather than "Oooh! My 1 single city has a Market now, and it's like, the year 500ad!" Again, a very well balanced, thought out game. But the lack of anything BIG was kind of a downer for me.

No oceans to cross, no new countries to discover...) Would have been better with a huge poster sized world map, less buildings, more cities that could be upgraded (grown) rather than improved through individual buildings. Missed the Civilzation across the ages and kept it smaller scale. Killed the theme for me.

In the couple of times playing, the game felt quiet balanced, which is a big up side. But I got it for the theme as well as the game and it just didn't work for us.

RATING: 9
 I think this is superb design that captures the feel of the popular computer game very well. The way combat is conducted with the 4 sided army cards and the variable stacking limits is uniquely brilliant, rewarding the person who invests heavily in a military strategy without making each battle a sure win. The way a tech-tree is built strikes the perfect balance between being overly complex and not having enough choice, and the production values in this game are top-notch. The main reason I only rate it a 9 out of 10 instead of the full 10 is because even at 5 hours, the game can feel a little prematurely truncated when you've been working towards one or two victory conditions the whole time and are almost there only to have victory snatched away by someone else fulfilling a different victory condition just ahead of you: this is a race to the finish! I'm also not entirely sure how balanced each of the winning conditions are yet. But I eagerly look forward to playing this a lot more in the future, and with the excitement building from my boys, that shouldn't be hard to do.

RATING: 7
My first real civilization game.

It was a good experience, but I was expecting a little more.

Complaints:

1. Setup takes too long.

2. My biggest complaint: It is too hard to keep track of all the powers that buildings, leaders, and wonders give you. I had a lot of moments where I regretted not doing a useful action which I missed because I didn't feel like checking 20 different card for useful effects at every moment.

No complaints:

1. Combat. Many don't like it. I did.

2. Complexity. Honestly, I don't think it is nearly as complex as people make out. I learned the rules very quickly.

3. Length. It's long, but given the type of game it is, what else would you expect?

RATING: 5
Another multiplayer conflict game from FFG. The game captures the flair of the computer game quite well, and it's commendable that with resources gathering, natives, tech trees, buildings, wonders, and of course combat the whole package stays fairly clean and housekeeping is kept very low-key.

Then again, you have your typical multiplayer conflict issues. It is once again a game where players need to keep each other in check until there is either kingmaking (whether through deliberate actions or carelessness), or until somebody has to win because everyone's just that one step away. There is also no way to win through generalization.

And even though Civilization integrates many secondary aspects the primary concern is moving your units around on a map. The resource gathering is extremely simplified, the tech trees are kind of disappointing with a lot of redundancy and nothing in the way of preconditions, and cultural advancements seem to be useful as a flanking strategy at most. What it comes down to, therefore, is military strength, either as a direct means for a military victory, or as a requisite defence and determent.

RATING: 7
Civilization: The Board Game has a lot of good ideas going for it.  I really like the 4 Victory conditions and that each and every one of them had other reasons to pursue it other than victory alone.  The tech pyramid idea has a nice growth feeling to it (though some of the names of the techs and their effects are so unrelated to each other).  The battle system is a bit wonky but I can't think of anything else like it and so it actually worked for me by and large.  Buildings and Wonder construction and effects were fine too.  But this game is so fiddly, with such a growing amount of text by the end, that it becomes a bit unapproachable.  I was quite enjoying it at the beginning as it is a rather comprehensive game that works very smoothly.  But by the end you have about 12 Techs each doing something else, 3 cities that have grown so much that you're constantly counting tiny little symbols.  A hand of Event cards that could be used at several key times and likewise you have to keep your eye on your opponents who have equally grown in immensity.  As such, there is a huge amount of mental bookkeeping that has to go on in order to make sense of the endgame state.  I think this would work just fine for those who play the game regularly and are blown away from the mechanics.  I myself, like the mechanics and I know that I won't be pulling this one out often due to the headache.  I've only played it 2-player and I can't imagine 4-player, which is certainly best (so there is a negotiation aspect) but I can't imagine the downtime and total game length here.  Just not what I'm in to.  Oh well- again, a fine game all things considered but just not my cup of tea for what I'm looking for in a civ game.  I guess I just like more Euro mechanics to avoid some of the sprawling mess that games like these can find themselves in.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Love all the civ computer games, love this game. Takes a long time [~90 min / person]. Totally worth it with the right people.

I have heard this gets significantly better with expansions (particularly WW). I will have to try that sometime.

The game could definitely use a few tweaks though! Here are the few tweaks I would make next time I play:
(1) All coin techs now read (max 3 coins) instead of (max 4 coins)
(2) 16 coins are required to win rather than 15 (i.e., turn dial back into the coin symbol)
(3) Barracks building provides no trade instead of 2 (final form, +2 combat)
(4) No wonder limit in a city - how un-Civ like is that?

Rationale:
(1) Economic victories are a little too easy. This helps slow it down a turn or two and makes the player actually plan. ;)
(2) See 1
(3) Barracks are the best trade buildings. That's just silly. Barracks are the best building period. Also silly. By the time you have academies, trade doesn't matter as much so they can stay.
(4) Seriously. Civ is all about building 10000 wonders in one megacity and ruling the world from there. Note that this does not elimiate the 1-starred building rule, that one is needed for balance. Lastly, this is also a minor boon to any culture victory pursuers out there, which probably isn't a bad thing. Time will tell.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Rating went up from 8 after further outings.

All in all, I'm very impressed by the game. It's a very well done conversion of the computer game - basic structure and goals are the same, yet the design is tight enough to keep the game in reasonable length. All the game mechanics seem to work and some - like the tech pyramid - are actually very clever. Calculating your best build for each city and deciding what tech to buy next can sometimes stall the game for a bit, but otherwise everything flows forwards very nicely.

The game length will certainly be an issue for some, but it's worth noting that gameplay itself feels both constantly engaging and surprisingly fast-paced. In the first game, I was surprised how soon all the players started to look like they were knocking on the door of one of the victory conditions. Shortly put, it does not feel nearly as long as it is. And with experienced players, our three-player games have clocked around 2-2,5 hours.

RATING: 4
This game is not bad or in any way broken, per se, it just has a lot of elements that I don't like about civ games.  Some offenders that come to mind:

-Overly involved mechanisms that increase play time but add little flavor.  For example, the combat mechanisms are reasonably complex but superfluous.

-Limited strategic options.  You have 4 paths to victory.  There are clear paths (techs, behavior) to get to each.  There aren't any creative or unexpected combos.

-Straightjacketed strategies based on initial choices.  This game initially seems to have a lot of choices in it, with a cool tech tree.  In practice, many of your strategy decisions are made for you the second you pick your civ.  Each civ is associated with a single type of victory.

Most importantly, I rate on the BGG scale---do you want to play it?  Answer is no.  Clash of Cultures completely fired this game.  I would never play this game over it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
So far this game has a lot of promise. It's a long game, so be prepared. There are enough opportunities to make bad decisions that new players should play an introductory, abridged game before diving in.

There's a lot to do in this game, lots of different paths to victory, and a ton of replay value. The game manages to offer a lot of meaningful decisions and ways to play, but also has been pared down so that no particular system in the game feels cluttered or bloated. 

Overall the game feels "worth it" despite the long play time, just make sure everyone in your group will be a good sport and see it through to the end.

RATING: 3
An intriguing early game leads into a disappointing mid and late game. We spent a lot of time scratching our heads while reading through the (typically FFG) rulebook. Once all the tiles had been explored, things got stale fast, and we were suddenly just buying technologies until we won the game. One of the biggest questions on our mind was why this game was designed with a maximum of 4 players in mind, when the source material is generally played with 7 civilizations. It felt like there would have been more here if we had had more ways to interact directly with eachother.

RATING: 8
I was eager to try this iteration of a Civ game at BGG.con.  When I finally found an open game starting up, I jumped in.  We all chose our starting civs, and set up for play.  In passing, several people who'd already played chimed in that my Russians and another player's Chinese were unable to win the Russians, and that was only because the Chinese player failed a capital assault by only one strength point earlier in the round.  The races and governments seemed very balaced and well differentiated, actually.  The Russians ability to steal technology, was also great.  It had me only a couple of turns from winning via technology.  

What is nice about the game is the multiple paths to victory.  Two of us pursued military (me tech as a secondary), while another came close via culture, and the fourth struggled the whole game, but only because he was being dogged by the Chinese player.  He was actually the only one to play before and explained the game, so theoretically had a better grasp of strategy.  

This is now on the wish list.  Look very much forward to playing again.

RATING: 7
Played at TotalCon. I was India, and I played thematically with Gandhi by focusing on culture and ignoring military. We didn't quite finish the game, but I think I was going to be able to pull out the victory. I had some culture cards that were going to help me avoid fights, and I also got lucky and had two generals from random great leaders.

The game is well designed. There are tons of interesting decisions to make, and I like that there are multiple paths to victory. The rules were rarely overwhelming, which is impressive considering the complexity of the game. It helped that we were playing with someone who was very familiar with the rules.

It felt like it took a really long time for our civilizations to start interacting, but that was mostly due to the time to explaining the rules, and slow first rounds as we figured out the mechanics.

All in all, it's one of those great games that I'll probably rarely play due to the length and complexity.

RATING: 6
The tech works, and the powers and victory conditions seem relatively balanced. That is no mean feat. That and the reasonable playtime (~3 hours) amounts to a significant accomplishment.
That said, though, the start player advantage on some turns is stupidly advantageous. As in, it is far too likely to determine the game's outcome. It is also the case that the game, for me, suffers from its immediate competitors--namely Antike and Through the Ages. Sure it's got a map (unlike TtA), but q.v. the turn order problems above, and it also introduces all the standard problems of multiplayer conflict games. In addition, the random map setup can hose players in terms of founding their crucial second city. So I'd rather take TtA for engine-building Civ goodness, and Antike for light Civ goodness with map-based conflict.
All in all, a near miss, but largely due to my own personal preferences.

RATING: 6.5
I think that this is an excellent reproduction of the computer game, with some (necessary) simplifications to reduce micromanagement and calculations.  However, it's still pretty long (it could use computerization!).  It would take a lot of practice to get a 4-player game down to 3 hours, I think.  But it might be easier to convince my game group to play this than Through The Ages, which has a lot of micro-management.

I hear that the "get 15 gold coins" strategy always seems to win.  It also seems that the military strategy is basically a distraction -- the attacker will cause the defender to waste time rebuilding defences, thus giving someone else the game.

It's interesting to note that although a winner is easy to determine, the remaining players probably don't have a "ranking", because there are different possible victory conditions.
