=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
There is a lot going on here, and a few games are required to grasp, let alone rate this title.

As a 2-player game, there is a runaway leader issue, but it's OK if all you want to do is learn the game.

As a 3-player game, it's OK if all three players have relatively the same experience level (ie. a new player might be an easy target for a Military victory).

As a 4-player game, the players are the balancing factor and can mitigate a runaway leader.  The game is also more forgiving if you are relatively new to the game.

With multiple paths to victory, and a lot of options, this is an excellent game, although some might find it too long.

Expect Analysis Paralysis from new players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Overall, this game delivers on what it sets out to do. As I understand it, it is to provide a rich, thematic, and mechanically sound civilization game that speaks to hundreds (if not thousands) of years of inter-, trans-, and anti-cultural development (and by anti-cultural, I mean way). This game provides multiple avenues to victory and a great deal of avenues to support them. The goals seem relatively balanced so long as everyone knows what to pay attention to and for (e.g., military build-up, cultural leaps and bounds) and is willing to meta-game (what would a game of civilization be without some table-talk?). For me, this game does take a long time but it doesn't feel long as it moves along at a good clip (so long as no-one at the table suffers from analysis paralysis) and since some of the goals' benchmarks can't be tampered with (to my knowledge, I'm talking about culture), the game clearly mounts towards a climax of some sort. Also, I find that the games aesthetics are clean, simple, functional, while still being nice to look at.

__________________

Let me start by saying that i admire how they kept all the good stuff of civ the computer game & simpliefied/modified the things you cannot translate into a board game.
This game gives you the same feel by offering you the four different ways of victory, via tech, military, culture & economy.
Even the fighting they implemented beautifully, with some bluffing & a decent portion of strategy.
My only one big drawback is that it's very long. Because although the game starts rather quickish, the turns are getting longer and longer, because you need to think about what to build/do in every of your 3 cities, what you need for that & where to put your pieces accordingly. And this is consecutively.

If you're a real buff & know what all the buildings & wonders & governments & techs do.. turns might be quicker but i doubt a game length under 4-5 hours. Add a person with AP and we're talking 6 hours. And that is a game length, that at least for me no game can make up for.
________________

Dropped from 7 to 6 for the length & the fact that it's hard to find that many dedicated players & the usual leaderbashing is kinda annoying.

I can't bring myself to rate Civ down, 'cos it is a great game. But I really kinda doubt that I'll ever play it again.

RATING: 4
I found it a bit too messy & have other games I would rather play when I want to play a long, empire building game (mostly those by Martin Wallace & some of those are short).  

Other thoughts:

Dice would be quicker & cleaner for combat.
The theme doesn't really work although I hear that's what the computer game is like.
Tech pyramid is a good idea although some of the techs are redundant.
Having multiple victory conditions is good, although I'm not sure how balanced they are.
There is a fair amount of bookkeeping & calculating levels of multiple resources which should have been more streamlined.

RATING: 4
Although it has a civilization theme, really this is an economic snowball game, where the idea is to gather resources, use them to buy 'buildings' that generate more resources etc. All of that means that a small early lead will turn into a massive lead by the end of the game, with the only levelling factor being the ability of players to attack one another. The knock on effect is that a tech victory is easily the best way to win as this creates a strong military while gunning for a cultural or financial victory will leave you too weak to resist when those enemies come to call. I've been deeply disappointed by this, you can be put out of contention quite early and there's still three hours to go!

RATING: 9
Prepare yourself for a long game - first timers should expect a game time of around 6 hours (especially once you include a quick pizza break!).  However, it's worth it - this game is pretty epic.

Far more tactics and depth than I could describe here, the game challenges you to turn your small band of scouts and warriors into a civilization that trumps your opponents in either technology, culture, wealth (sort of) or military clout.

The main things I really want to say about this game, is that I really enjoyed the fact that although the game is very long, you don't notice much downtime.  There's lots to talk about with the other players when someone is taking their turn, and often you can take actions to block another player's movements when it's not your turn also.

A massive game with epic replay potential, grab some beers, pizza and some mates and get stuck into this brilliant game.

(Quick recommendation - get everyone to read the rules the day before you play, it will make the setup and explanation much easier)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Well, well, what do you know. Kev & FF get it right afterall, mostly. I think its probably their best game since TI3 AND it might be better AND could even be their best game....I even bought a copy after playing and I barely buy any new games these days. Not too fiddly as I worried it could be, at least for FF and as I mentioned before in my pre-comments a good CIV game can't avoid being at least a little or it wouldn't be a true CIV game, play was mostly intuitative and flowed well too. 

Now note that I have only played once so far and it was the short game with 3 players, which I think hit a sweet spot, at least for me, was about 2.5 hours and I know the next game would be 2 for sure and I had this confirmed by some other players. Also, I am an old fan of the computer game and I can tell you that [i]this game brought back some memories[/i], yes, playing does bring out the feel of the old beloved. (I also have not yet played TTA oddly enough so can't compare)

The short game does concern me though, in particular the Culture track seems way too hard to climb for the win, more experience? or maybe a tweak needed there, not a biggy anyway. I also wonder if the income track is a bit on the easy side but I think the 3 other than culture are close. Wonders seemed hard to get in the short game as well, really the game is just ramping up when the short game ends which really makes it almost a different game and really with the rules as written you will need the full game to bring everything to fruitation (a tweak to the short game might be to start off a little advanced or something to mimic the full game better, more thought on this after more plays...). 

Combat, well this was the weak point of the game for me and maybe the only significant one, other than the length of the full game which I hear is about 4 hours. It started off bad when units were randomly given out (why not give equal to start?) and then the rock, paper, scissors aspect was a bit alarming. At the beginning of the game the first fights were silly really, just tit for tat and whoever got dealt the crappier units lost (usually you against a tribe at beginning) BUT it did seem to improve as the game progressed with upgraded units, more units, other bonuses etc. So right now, this is my big question mark with the game, we did not have a lot of combat since it takes a while to ramp up and this is another concern with the short game as by the time you do ramp it up, someone will be ready to win somewhere. I think the short game could use a few small tweaks (more plays to confirm though), but then, I think most games could... ;) or worded another way, most games [i]improve[/i] with some small tweaks. 

Anyway the rest of the game was really well done I thought and very enjoyable. Wonders are really cool, the tech tree was really cool(might want to take out the NUKE card), buildings give more resources and are upgradable, the income/trade dial was a cool design, the culture track (those cards though?), multiple paths with 4 ways to win, mostly lower luck (combat has it, not sure how much yet though, shouldn't be too bad and it would seem even in the full game combat is a more minor element in the game) so overall I was quite pleased with the game on one playing of the short game given my hesitations with FF at the helm of this, its a very strategic game actually (you can plan your tech tree start to finish for one example) and I look forward to my next game. 

[u]Update:[/u] 8.5 => 8 Played the full game 2 player. It was LONGGGG and goodness two player only. I guess we were slow players, really there is enough look ahead that the game just keeps you thinking, this is great but has the negative effect that goes with it. 

I think I am okay with combat mostly and how it works, once the game gets past the initial stage. Not sure about when someone's city gets snuffed out though in the later stage, boy that sure is hard to recover from and what is with Atomic Energy and just nuking a city because you randomly found the right token under a tribe?  Nahh, we put that silly Tech out, I like the theme behind it but now how it was implemented or the effects!  

I think I am okay with the Culture cards even as they mostly just attack buildings as their one screwage element which isn't too severe. I don't like the tradeoff of one of them, getting a resource and it gives someone else a coin, and I don't like the Tech that when you get 9 trade value someone else gets 6 (should have been 4?) but these are very minor nitpicks. Overall I think the luck level is fine except for the Nuke Tech.  

I can see a Combat issue in the Multiplayer game for sure where attacking someone and depleting your forces leaves you and the person you attacked wide open. So while I think how combat works is okay I just don't know how well the results of Combat sit with me are, whether someone loses a City late in a long game and can't recover and/or whether the first person to have a major fight leaves himself and the defender vunerable, opening the door for the lying cat. This and the game length are really my only issues with the game, but they are kinda big ones. I REALLY like the rest of the game though so I'm torn. I am thinking about a modified version of the short game that somewhat simulates the full game a little more.

Finally had a chance to play this at Origins and my feelings on it were definitely up and down. I was a tad PO'd at this point and it took quite a few turns to get units and armies back. Just an hour into the game and I had decided that I really didn't like this game and I in fact wished I could quit. 

Luckily I stuck with it, learned the game as we went and figured out how I would play the NEXT time. I even somehow mananged to win despite getting way behind early on. In the end I did like the game, it was just marred by a very bad rules explanation.

**One of those rares things where I've only played once and it wasn't even the greatest experience, yet I can't stop thinking about the game five days later.  Reread the rules and moved my rating up a bit.**

Later got this in a trade and I have to say that the only thing keeping this from being rated higher is the steep learning curve. Not that the game is hard, it just has a lot of little rules that are easy to forget and the combat system is so....wonky.

**UPDATE**After seven plays I have to say that this is a pretty fantastic game.  The combat system is still a little odd but it's the thing holding this game from being a 10 rating.

RATING: 8
Very enjoyable Civ game.  Explore the board, build three cities, get tech, build improvements to the cities, fight armies, multiple paths to victory.  Can win by conquering a home city, or by winning the tech race, or by winning the culture race, or by winning the coin race.  Has tech fun and city building fun of civ games.  Exploration but only for the first couple rounds.  Spatial maneuvering actually rather limited since terrain irrelevant once one has navigation.  Combat is a bit clunky.  Sense of strategizing not well defined somehow, compared to Through the Ages.  Afterwards I can think of plays I could have done better but not really creatively thinking of interesting strategies.  More about responding well to what your neighbor is doing and making good tech and city building choices where it's not necessarily clear what the best choices were even afterwards but it's satisfying to have things build up.

RATING: 9
--2011/01/08--
A great game. Very nice mechanics for an American Game. So far it's my favourite one. It has a lot of variety but it must be played with an appropriate group since it's not an easy game and a rookie may unbalance strenght ratios. Masterpiece!

UPDATE
--2012/11/06--
After many plays I've rated it down by 1 because I consider its rating as an average with the expansion's one. The base-game was great (apart from minor issues which could have been fixed in a different way by the expansions). Unfortunately the expansion hadn't changed anything but worsening the game itself. Even if it gives more choices by introducing ancient ruins, great people and new civs, it unbalances the game dangerously. Buildings aren't useful anymore, as it's mostly a waste of time spending an action(unacceptable for a Civ game). It could have been the best civ game so far, but the expansion faded the theme of the evolution (initially not so strong I have to admit) away.

UPDATE
--2013/11/06--
Wisdom and Warfare is the key!!! Finally this game has been fixed and it is now working very well. A lot of balancing issues have been fixed (thanks to the bggcommunity too), tech victory is now viable.
I strongly suggest not to play the game without the second expansion as it's TOTALLY BROKEN ! It's better to play the base game instead than the Base game + F&F.

Rating can finally go back to 10!

UPDATE 
--- 14/03/2015 ---
Rating lowered to 9. Great game but too much randomness for my taste to deserve a 10.

RATING: 6.5
2012-05-18

Initial Rating: 8.5 (April 2012)

Some friends have told me negative things about the game so it took me nearly a year to play it, I was sure it was going to be a very bad game. Boy I was very surprised!! Civilization is a great game, I just upgraded the rating to a 9.0 and I can´t wait to play it again.

As a fan of the computer games, I feel the board game is a very good implementation of the game system, it can take a while to play but the game flows so quickly you don`t feel the time. I enjoy it immensely.  

The game has a lot of replayability because each time you play with different tiles (and different orientation), different civilizations, different technologies etc.  It has a medium luck factor due to the units and cultural cards but it doesn´t bother me, perhaps the combat is the hardest concept to understand but not so hard.

All in all a great game and a must buy for me.

Right now it is my second favorite civilization game after Through the Ages.

RATING: 7
Wow.  Kevin Wilson and the FFG team have managed to create a game that recreates faithfully the feel of the PC games, but plays in a very manageable 1 hour per player.  A completely immersive experience and thanks to the innovative technology paths, a game that seems to have a substantial amount of replayability.

I’m very pleased that FFG decided to put Civilization out at the $50 price point instead of making it a big-box game with lots of plastic.  This game is a fantastic value, just like BSG and Arkham Horror before it.

I have minor balance concerns; tech victory seems hard to attain.  I even tried for one in my second game before switching gears to coins midway through.  I am pleased that I’ve seen the other three different victories in my first three plays; that bodes well.

Edit:  I've downrated this game as I'm less than pleased with the "king of the hill" multiplayer conflict aspect it brings out.  I want to rate it higher but I don't have a lot of enthusiasm for it at the moment.

RATING: 2
Rating based on one 4p play only. NB I haven't played the video game, so this is based purely on how I found the board game.

Turn by turn game play is smoother than I had feared after reading the rules, but it's still a very fiddly spreadsheet-like affair that comes down to efficiency and optimisation without a great deal of interaction, at least until someone kicks off a military campaign in the later stages of the game. You need to invest a fair amount of time learning the various buildings, techs and wonders, how they relate to each other, and how to move through them as efficiently as possible to play well.

I'd challenge the 'quick playing' tag, it's still a long game that for me dragged in the second half.

Also not sure about the military strategy, as it seems strong and (even it it doesn't provide a winner) the player the militarist targets is likely to be knocked out of the running. Fine in a game of Risk where it's all about fighting, less so if you've spent 2 hours focussing on (say) culture and have to take 3 turns out of culture vulturing to ready some sort of defence against an aggressor. Those 3 turns not developing culture will likely put you out of the running for no reason other than you're sat next to the German or Russian player.

RATING: 4

As a civilization board game it ticks many of the right boxes, which makes me curious as to why it did not engage me more.  In the end its just kind of ponderous. The tech pyramid is a clever abstraction, but without genuine prerequisites it lacks something.  The whole battle mechanism sounds cool but is actually a bit dull.  If you run out of combat cards then you are really fodder - make sure you sit to the left of the new player so you can take their capital when they lose their units.  In the end, the game is just too dull.  Civ needs to move quicker than this.

RATING: 6.5
Perhaps this is common for games of this length, but it feels like Civ is terrifically inflexible. Set your strategy, and hope that it carries you through to a win - because changing your mind mid-game isn't a viable option if your opponent is doing well. And spending your game trying to upset the leader isn't all that enjoyable when it takes 3 hours. If, halfway through the game, you see that you're behind, it's excruciating to have to play out the next hour and a half knowing you can't affect the final outcome. 

On another note, I hate the combat system; why they didn't use dice, I have no idea.

Altogether, not a bad game. Just one that I can't see myself playing much.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
FFG's release manages to capture the spirit and feel of the fantastic computer game in a boardgame format. The best feature is a feature which is rarely seen in multiplayer conflict games. It is possible to score a non-military win although military can't be neglected totally as otherwise your civilization will be the playground for the nations with strong armies. As usual there will be the customary leaderbashing towards the end but it pays out that there are multiple ways to victory and not everything can be taken away.
It's a bit on the fiddly side but compared to Through the Ages it stays manageable and it's far easier to follow your opponents actions. With the inclusion of a map it takes a more conventional approach to civ-building than TTA and is more Ameritrashy than its competitor.
Statistics indicate that some of the cultures have slightly better chances at winning and military or economic victories are more often. As with all asymmetric multiplayer games the game balance is in the hands of the players and Civilization should be able to show its real potential with experienced players.
After one play, it met my comparably high expectations for a four-hour multiplayer game.

RATING: 8
I played this once when it came out, and then the second time a mere seven years later, but that's no fault of the game itself. I love 4xs, especially with a civ theme, so I own and enjoy Clash of Cultures and Through the Ages, among others. Compared to CoC, this one's a little baroque, and the rulebook is prolix in the old Kevin Wilson/FFG way that we'll probably never see again, but the gist is quite easy, leaving the focus primarily on gameplay. Again relative to CoC, SMC is less streamlined, but I have found the granularity of this appealing as an alternative to rather than a replacement for CoC. I even kind of like the combat system, which should be taught and thought of as a minigame, and WaW probably makes it better.

RATING: 8.5
Pos-things:
Great game, love the theme, everything works out, the changing map and the random civilization brings variability to the game. Not too much text in the game. The artwork is great and detailed. It has a very different and unique combat system. 
 Neg-things:
Sometimes a lot of rules to remind, a very long game, especially if you consider too much possibilities. Is a game that overwhelms new board gamers. I just recommend this game if you know that someone else will want to play with you. It seems to me that not all the wining conditions have the same effort to achieve, economic victory it’s for me the easiest.

Cosas-pos:
Gran juego, me encanta la temática, todo encaja muy bien. El mapa cambiante y la civilización otorgada al azar le da mucha variabilidad al juego. El juego no tiene tantísimo texto. El arte del tablero y las fichas es también muy bonito y detallado. Tiene un modo de combate diferente y único.
Cosas-neg:
En ocasiones son demasiadas reglas para recordar en un determinado momento. El juego es muy largo, sobre todo la primera vez que lo juegas. Es un juego que satura a jugadores de mesa nuevos. Sólo recomiendo este juego si sabes que tendrá alguien con quién jugar. Me parece que no todas las condiciones de victoria son igual de completables, la victoria económica me parece más fácil de lograr.

RATING: 7
Rating based on solo play to learn mechanics and explore some of the strategies.

I'm a big fan of Civ games, starting with AH's version in the '80s, several editions of the PC game and Advanced Civ.  I've played Through the Ages and the Eagle Game of the same name as this.

This game could be the Civ lite I've been looking for.  You have armies on the map and a tech tree.  There are multiple paths to victory and the limits to armies and cities mean this game isn't going to take forever.

I worry that poor tile draws with poor city sites may constrain some players, but there are always military solutions to that!  It will be interesting to see how this holds up to multiple plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Sid Meier's Civilization (2010) is a whole lot of game. It tasks you with building a winning strategy from a large pool of game mechanics, as well as a variety of win conditions. In the game, you build cities and structures (including wonders of the world), explore new territory, fight NPC villagers, deal with inter-player politics, and more. First time players may feel overwhelmed by the sheer number of possible things to do in any given turn, but those looking for strategic depth and a wealth of possibilities from any one session are nearly certain to enjoy this one.

In any game, a winner will be decided via military conquest, or via economic, cultural or technological superiority. The fact that there are multiple separate win conditions that can be pursued is straight from the computer game from which the game takes its inspiration (mainly, for this version, Civilization IV). The road to any victory is a long one, and the game rewards players who dabble in a bit of everything. New technologies give players stronger military units and new ways to grow their economy; a stronger economy reduces the effective cost of gaining new technology; focusing on culture grants players special action cards that can be used to assist every aspect of the game. Naturally, one also needs to have a mind towards military development, if for nothing else but defense.

Each player plays as a specific civilization: the core game includes the Americans, Chinese, Germans, Romans, Russians and Egyptians. Each of these gets powerful special bonuses; Russians can steal technology, Germans get free military units, Americans have boosts to production, Romans get free culture advancement for doing specific things, Egyptians get free buildings, and the Chinese start with higher defense in their capital. Each player also has a specific government that can be unlocked with technologies that add to specific areas of their civ. The combinations of different civs in any one game can shape the power dynamic dramatically.

The board for the game is a randomized modular board built from 4x4 square tiles. The random nature of the board combined with the different civs in play only dramatically multiplies the variety that any one game can contain. Just in the core game, I've found a level of replayability that I don't often find in games of this complexity and time investment. The production value is also fantastic; the multitude of square tokens reminds me of game designer Kevin Wilson's other creation (and personal favorite), Arkham Horror.

It's not for everyone, as the game generally runs long and there are a lot of different things to consider on a turn, which might be overwhelming. With the right group, however, Civilization is hard to beat. The different avenues of play make repeat plays and experimentation very rewarding. My general play experience has been 1v1 with my wife, and while we stand at a 3/1 record, even when I lost I had a great time playing, and my wife is seemingly always willing to play more. When a competitive game is fun and rewarding even when you lose, you know you have something special.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
At first view a well done CIV-Game. Far better than the older version from Eagle Games. 

Sadly a bit too abstract in my opinion regarding military confrontations and the small amount of armies and cities. You really don't have the feeling to build an empire and you can't really hinder one's movement because you don't have enough army tokens to block movement.

But here is one REAL bad point: The combat system sucks. It feels like someone simply wanted to avoid the good old dice and make something especially elegant - but in reality the combat system only feels artificial, random and makes no sense when trying to explain the rules with real-life-situations. AND it is a time killer - it takes much too long to fight out a simple little battle.

Without this crap of combat system the game could have made it to an "8", so it`s only a "6". Everyone of our gaming group likes this civ game but hates the combat system, so we often find us playing another game with civ background.

RATING: 9
Amazingly "tight" for a civilization game, made so by very carefully omitting certain aspects (such as happiness, and more than three cities) which had been modelled before and highlighting others which had been somewhat skimmed over (tech trees, individual buildings in cities, great leaders, cultural events) in recent offerings.

This is probably the most successful of the spate of Civilization-type games I've played in the last 5-7 years, and although it has its weaker points (combat is still oversimplified, and the world feels more like a fishbowl than an entire planet), it's a really good design that hits all the right aspects of a civilization building game - the classic 4x model "eXplore, eXpand, eXploit, eXterminate", while allowing for many possible paths to victory.

RATING: 5.5
This straddles the game between board and war game (which is good).  In the same genre whoever, I'd MUCH rather play Runewars.  

This Civilization is, although much faster than the previous, still waaay too long and slow, with tons of downtime (maybe better with less than 4?). =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
In summary, it is a resource management game. There are a lot of interesting mechanics in there if you intend to design civ like game of your own. There are also interesting similarity with the video game, so similar that they even transferred the bugs.

In addition to that, I got screwed badly in the game I played. So it does not help to make an objective review. In my case, a player blocked me the possibility to create a third city. Since my trade was very low and I was getting 2/3 the resource of the other players, I fell way back behind with no possibility to win. So as a revenge, I played king making on the 3rd player to make him win.

First, the pacing of the game is very slow and the map design is not interesting. So when you want to move to attack your opponent, it is not as easy as I would like. Personally, I would have unconnected the board. Instead of moving units on the board, you simply select your target city and attack.

Second, your production is the only resource that cannot be split on multiple action (1 technology allows you to split in half). I had placed a city with a lot of production (because in civ games, it is normally good) but I realized that it is completely useless. Of course, I could build a wonder, but I can only build 1 wonder per city. Else I have to destroy my own wonder to build a 2nd one (totally stupid)

The problem is that each city has 1 action. So even if your city could have 10000 production, it does not matter because you can only produce 1 thing. (2 with engineering). All other resources like trade and culture are cumulative, so 10000 of these would actually break the games. This means if you are in a bad situation, you cannot shift all your production to troop creation because you can only create 1 unit per turn. Not only you must produce figures but also units. So you need like 2 or 3 actions to place 1 unit capable of fighting into play. You get in a situation where you must never lose your stating units because it is very hard to recover.

There is also some technology which are essential to make the game work. Irrigation allows you to give a 3rd city. Like I said above, it is impossible to win with 2 cities. So you have a light dominant strategy where everybody must get irrigation. It is possible to win without engineering, but 2 action per city per turn somewhat doubles your production capacity. Personally, I would set that as the default rule.

The other thing I did not liked is that you must focus on a single victory condition through all the game. In the civilization video game, you can normally change your focus in the middle of the game, but in this board game, it is impossible. The worst thing, is that the civilization you take will dictate what should be your victory condition. If you do not focus for that victory condition, you are screwed.

Finally, the board game has a bug in particular that I don't like which is present in all video game. I call it the concept of having technology research proportional to empire size. That has the effect of creating some sort of runaway leader syndrome where that player cannot be defeated anymore. This can generally be solved by making technological research not depend on empire size. 

Still, in this board game, players can at most build 3 cities, so everybody has the same empire size, but if a city get's destroyed or not built, you simply place that player out of the game.

So I am really not impressed by the game play. It's a good source of inspiration for my design but that's all. I prefer playing age of mythology instead since the board are not connected and since you always stay competitive even if bad things happen to you.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
LIKES:

* Diverse tech paths that support different victory conditions bring interesting variation into the game.
* Tiles for land is different each game as they can rotate in differently each time.  No land mass will ever be the same.
* Four different win conditions allows for different and interesting paths to victory and guides one's tech pursuit.
* I like the combat system with the paper, rock, scissors concept and slight deck-building aspect.
* I appreciate that as you learn higher techs, your lower level buildings and army units upgrade immediately!
* Game is streamlined fairly well although there is still a fair bit of accounting that occurs.

DISLIKES:

* Games can take a bit longer than I care to play in general.  Outside of conflict, the game is largely solitaire so waiting on someone else can feel frustratingly long at times.  This almost kills the game for me.
* There is a lot of finicky counting up of icons each turn for both production and trade which can get old in a hurry.  
* The culture and gold chits are pretty small and can be hard to handle.  
* I'm not certain if it bothers me or not, but some of the cultural events are rather chaotic and mean in nature.  Kingmaking is something that can and often will easily occur in this game.
* This is a minor annoyance, but for a FFG, I'm disappointed in the lack of plastic miniatures...;-)  And wimpy flags to represent armies?  Really?

RATINGS CHANGES:  7 -> 6

Ultimately, I'd just prefer to play the game on the computer and/or play Through the Ages so I sold the game.

RATING: 2
Exceedingly disappointing. As a joyous fan of the digital series, I found Kevin Wilson's analog conversion lacking. I attribute the most glaring inequities in the game -- the overly dominant Economic (and to a lesser extent Military) Victory, the utterly infeasible Culture (and to a lesser extent Technology) Victory, the overly dominant Egyptian and Russian Civilizations, the utterly helpless and hapless American and Roman Civilizations, the utterly unbalanced Wonders (...Hanging Gardens, anyone?) -- to a complete lack of playtesting.

Let us be discretely honest, now: did anyone at FFG actually play this prior to shipping? I have profound doubts.

That said, the aesthetic design is of FFG's customary brilliance. But colorful frills, meeple bells, and plastic whistles do not a good board game make.

You're zero for two, Kevin. Better luck next time.

Humbly yours,
leycec

RATING: 9.43
Comments entered on 17 May 2012:

Sid Meier's Civilization: The Board Game has been released for about a year and half at this point. This game has the major themes from the computer game but borrows heavily from Sid Meier's Revolution.

I have been a huge fan of the Sid Meier's Civilization computer game series for about 20 years. I have played every incarnation and was very happy to see FFG get a chance to develop a board game from this license. It was a great bonus that Kevin Wilson was the designer for this game, as I have had a chance over the past year to relay my games played to him and get his feedback on the game and how we have been playing.

I wanted to get a chance to see how each Civilization fared against each other Civilization in a head-to-head match. Aaron Lynch has been willing to partake in this grand experiment. As of this entry, Aaron and I have played 29 two-player games.

RATING: 7
This is essentially Civ Revolution translated into board game form.

While I've read comments as to "why would you do that?" I would say this: There are a number of major elements that are different enough to say this is a different experience albeit heavily derivative of the source material. Further the human interaction in the game makes any comparison impossible.

There truly is the diversity of choices and strategy with just enough randomness in the system to create a new experience with every play, requiring changes in strategy every time.

Expect initial plays to be in the four hour range though I expect that to drop dramatically with experience, weighing in at 45 minutes a player at the utmost.

Update:

Length and rules overhead and reduced the "return to" rate, and therefore this drops to a 7.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
When we were about three hours into our first game, this looked like a winner and surefire purchase to me: multiple paths to victory, a brilliant technology tree, short and punchy actions, cool combat ... another hit out of the park for FFG.

After another couple of hours, in which I was hit by several droughts in spite of having built the UN, and had lost a couple of my own culture cards and oodles of trade to no less than three Writing action nerfs, I was more willing to punch Kevin Wilson on the nose. Especially since we strongly suspected that something was awry, but neither the rulebook nor the official FAQ that we consulted during the game helped.

Thus, the game gets docked three points from the nine it would have deserved with proper card editing:
- one for the sloppiness itself
- another one because FFG had really shaped up its act with Runewars and Android, games that are playable right out of the box without any similar hassles and rule corrections
- and yet another one because those three Writing nerfs really bloody hurt.

Update
A couple more plays allowed me to overcome my initial misgivings - apart from the sloppiness described above, this is a very tight and good game, whose greatest strength lie in streamlining the parts that could have led less careful designers into a fiddliness nightmare (techs and armies, especially) and in balancing the various victory paths.

I say the latter although we’ve only seen military or monetary victories so far, both due to what I’ve read elsewhere and to how those games went. Both kinds of win require a bit of carelessness from your opponents; they have to leave you alone early in the game for a monetary win, and somebody has to neglect their defensive strengh to lose his capital and grant you a military win. I’m pretty sure that more experienced game groups will see more cultural and tech wins, especially since both these paths feed strongly into everything else you do: winning without some key technologies is impossible no matter what you do, and cultural events are crucial in blocking others from the win and protecting your finishing moves.

All in all, this is probably Kevin Wilson’s most accomplished design to date - not his best or his most original (both slots would be filled by Android, in my opinion), but the one with the best balance and most seamless play (apart from some initial hurdles to overcome ...).

RATING: 7
First impressions are this is an outstanding game.  The rules are excellent, leaving very few questions after the first game played.  Quality of components also good, not as flashy as the FFG big box games (hence, it is the small box format).

Quibbles are exceptionally minor.  The trade/coin dials are inferior to the Runewars setup.  The battle cards have minor design issues - tiny icons, green/blue hard to discern on the tiny icons, asymmetrical backs.

Tech tree implementation is great.  Game provides a tremendous amount of replay just in the tech part alone - more than twice as many techs available as normally used in a single game.

After 7 plays this game is outstanding.  The balance and the replayability are great for a 4P game.  We can now play in 2 hours without a problem.  Civ in 2 hours, tough decisions, replayability, and FFG quality.  This is looking like a new 10 for us.

The game is a 4P game.  3P is okay.  2P is a pass.  Each plays a bit differently.

Latest word - dropped from 10 to 9 due to scaling.  Only the 4P works for me.  And now another drop to an 8 to make room for Clash of Cultures which I prefer to this one.

2015 - after some 3P plays dropped rating to a 7. These really did not impress. Will try again someday with 4, but the game is about played out.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I like the "Age of Empires" feel to it, but it feels like the game takes way too long for what it is.  The turns go by relatively quickly, but they also feel clunky.  Mary and I aren't really prone to AP, but this game brings it out.

[u][geekurl=/thread/979221/quick-introduction-componentssetup-verdict]Written Review[/geekurl][/u]

[COLOR=#0033FF]Had: Fame & Fortune, Wisdom & Warfare[/COLOR]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The variable player powers are fun. Researching technologies is fun. Gaining rewards through culture is fun. Exploring the map is fun. The basic mechanic of collecting resources from the land around your cities is much like in the PC game, and that aspect of things also works well.

On the downside, the entire army and battle system is a bit boring and takes up too much focus in the game. The cards and upgrade tokens are fiddly and you never feel like you're in solid control over your military. The battles themselves are also a bit boring.

The other downside is that this game is really long and you spend a lot of time waiting around for other people to take their city actions. Our group's first game was called to a halt after 4 hours when it was approaching 1am and there was still no immediate winner in sight. Granted, much of that time was spent looking up rules, because oh man there are a lot of them.

After playing a few more games, we now "get it" and the gameplay moves a little faster, thankfully. But it still has a tendency to outstay its welcome in the last 40 minutes or so.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
everything fits and it works.  compared, somewhat unfairly, to Through the Ages.   TTA is a much better 2p game and potentially 3p as well, but this Civ is a better 4p experience.   I think the game was designed as a 4p as there is a nice natural alliance for opposite players and better trading opportunities that do not really exist otherwise.

3p variant posted here is nice and creates a more balanced board.  trading is not really present and there is heavy bash the leader.

initial play or two takes far far longer than the game will take once those are under the belt which might turn off some groups.  i almost think the playtime of those initial plays will be 1.75x or even 2x.

***** update Jan 2010

still interesting.  potential kingmaker issues during the last turn of the game, but the overall experience is enough that it does not bother me.  takes at least 2 or 3 plays to get a feel for the different victory conditions.  odds are that your playgroup will initially sway to one of the four and it will take a period for players to adjust to the other options.

3p works with the map variant (posted in the variants section), but the game really is a 4p game.  if a player ends up dueling with both opponents, they will likely be climbing a steep hill back into the game to compete for a victory.

trying 2p this coming Sunday for an hour just to see if it is remotely interesting, but I expect this will be the only 2p game I play of this.

**************

2p about as expected.  neat way to try out different things.  coins great and certainly an interesting strategy.  hard to get really cranked up, but might be the hardest to deal with in late game (regarding preventing victory).

do not really get comments about tech/military.  tech very much functions as a game clock as essentially eveyone will end up 0-2 turns from a tech "victory" at game end if the game ends in this manner.

**************

given the very abstractedness of military combined with the map - i think the design works just fine.

RATING: 9
Xmas 2010
Rating after first solo play. Incredibly well balanced, which surprised me. Almost TOO many choices to make from turn-to-turn. A far superior Civ experience that TTA for a number of reasons: significantly less fiddly, what FFG Civ lacks in micromanagement, it makes up for in a tactical layer, and for a number of ways to effect one's opponent besides merely military (something I feel is severely lacking in TTA.) Easier to master than TTA (but only barely so), artwork and design better than TTA.

I can see this one really growing with repeated plays.

---------------------------------------

Ratings bump to 9 after a three player game that came down to victory in the final turn. Those that complain there is not enough player interaction are simply not playing it right. TONS of difficult and interesting choices that create a web of strategic effects later in the game, and a tremendous amount of balance between the various victory paths. Can't wait to play it more.

RATING: 7
OK, let me start by saying that this is a good game, with a lot of things going on and a lot to consider on your turn.  I would have rated it an 8, but our 1st play took 6 hours with setup and rules explanation.  It's very difficult for me to rate any game that takes over 4 hours higher than a 7.  I mean a rating of 8 has at the ending of it's description "and will never turn down a game".  I know that with more plays (with the same group of people) the time would probably drop to 4 hours or less, but I just don't see this happening any time soon.

Of course there are exceptions.  I mean Twilight Struggle and Through the Ages can take over 4 hours as well, but I've rated these higher.  Maybe someday I will raise this game's rating as well after more plays.  But is that really going to happen?  I highly doubt it.  Reason being is that it will just not see the table with our group but on a rare occasion (i.e. holiday / weekend gaming... which is once or twice a year at best).  And to be honest with you, even though this game has beautiful components and map compared to TtA's spartan & drab presentation, most of the time I would prefer TtA to this game.  TtA just feels more intuitive and steamlined, even without the map (probably because it doesn't have a map).

So I may change my mind in the future and bump this game up in rating (don't really see it going down), but for now I leave it at a respectable rating of 7.

RATING: 10
3 quick plays so far. Amazingly detailed game, achieves the intentions of Civilization extremely well.

Obviously, it's FFG so parts and looks are amazing.

Each victory system seems viable, only initial concern is that an optimised coin build seems a bit too strong.

This is a game that suffers badly in assymetric information environments - if players know more or less about the core technology decks than the others at the table, they will be at a significant advantage/disadvantage. This is not a game for the uninitiated.

It's also quite hard to teach to a newcomer, but it is very teachable by playing a few rounds (unlike some games which can't avoid lengthy briefings).

I will update based on further plays and a highly competitive 4 player game I'm planning later this week... if it stands up to pro play, I'll probably give it a 9.

Great game.

---------------
Update after 10 plays.

Game is still fantastic - This is a well built game that offers a VERY wide array of strategy and builds.

Only concerns so far:

- Russia is overpowered. They are good at frickin' everything. They shouldn't be given Communism from Turn 1, it's too powerful.

- Culture is weak. Once you get experienced players at the table, military conflict is constant and escalating. Culture can't compete in that space because it has to stop harvesting culture to build units/armies.

- Tech victories are only possible with Russia or with early culture cards to exchange ideas. Tech needs at least 15 turns without acceleration. The game is usually over by turn 11-12.

-------------

Okay, so we've ticked over to 15 plays. This game is amazing. Seriously, this is one of the greatest games ever made, IMHO.

Comments regarding the clunkyness or the spamminess are misplaced. This is a beautiful, rich, deep and rewarding game experience and I have found a use for every single one of its beautiful components.

I'm bumping this to 9.5

-----
Screw it. This is my 10.

It's the only game I'm giving this to, and mainly because the game works just as well with a variant we are really enjoying, where you ignore all racial effects and all players simply select a turn-one first tier technology of their choice.

A 10 can only go to a game that meets the highest standards of theme, component quality, mechanics, design, balance, flow, concept and strategic/tactical legitimacy. As far as I'm concerned, I can think of no game which comes close to this one on virtually every one of those fronts and for the 'empire' or 'civilisation' game category, this is truly the masterpiece.

Never gets old, everyone loves to play it, a beautiful game.

10/10 and well earned.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After about 10-12 sessions, here's my verdict: 

With a regular group of enthusiasts, it could eventually become one of those very few personal all time favourites, but it has issues. It's unbalanced; it's unthematic in some aspects (combat system; no terrain effects on movement or combat, limits for wonders and star buildings...). Also, it doesn't really feel like you're building a civilization. It's a race for your designated victory condition, and it always comes down to military. It's a 4x game with a civilization theme, and by far the best way to play it is with straigh in-your-face aggressiveness. I don't mind that at all, but new players may be disappointed. 

More thoughts: It's prone to AP, but mitigation is possible. For example, allow spending culture at any time in order to draw cards before your city management phase. Prepare help sheets, especially for counting resources like hammers/city. Real playing time: ~1.5 hours/player. Record: 8 hours for a 3p session, pure awesomeness! 

Compared to other contemporary civs, I currently like this one the most. If TtA is a classic symphony, then that game is Motorhead.

RATING: 8.5
Playing this game turned out to be quite an experience!

First, this game is not for everyone, it's truely for board game geeks. However, once you get passed the first one or two hours required to assimilate the endless game rules and get to know the wealth of content, this game turns into a total blast, that will totally make you forget that you've been playing for 5 straight hours.

Players are faced with very interesting and meaningful choices at every turn. The multiple ways of winning provide a chance for every player to get back from behind. This however is less true in 1 vs 1 games, making 3 players and 4 players games much more interesting.

RATING: 8
規則:
*市場圖板放置好，四種部隊卡份類洗勻放置對應位置，建築物標記也放對應位置，奇觀卡照時代分類洗勻疊起，開四張放對應的標記在上面(埃及玩家隨機抽一，之後市場不一張)，文化事件卡也照牌背洗勻放在文化軌對應標誌下，每有一個玩家就每種市場資源放一個到市場圖板上，其他標記洗勻蓋著放置，戰鬥獎勵卡跟科技卡5放一旁
*每人選則或抽一個文明圖板，選一個顏色的配件，拿一個首都個兩個城市建築標記，跟一套政體卡，每人拿飛行器已外部隊卡各一張蓋著放一旁，拿最低等級的軍事標記放市場圖板對應部隊卡下方，飛行器的標記則星號朝下放，文化標記放在文化軌0的位置，每人拿取對應文化的起始版塊打開放面前，中立板塊洗勻照人數排放組成對應地圖，隨機起始，每個玩家放置首都在自己起始圖板合法位置(水域不行，箭頭處為建議位置)，並獲得起始文明獎勵，放置一個偵查隊跟一個軍隊在城市周遭八格內(水上不行)，並放置起始科技跟起始政體卡(正常是專制)寄送市郊(周圍八格)有多少貿易圖示轉幾格自己圖板貿易軌，錢幣軌也根據市郊或科技調整
*遊戲進行多輪每輪有五個階段，直到有人文化軌到底/科技到5/錢幣到15/打下別人首都則獲勝
*遊戲個過程中偵查隊所在格的所有標記都可視為某城市市郊，被敵軍佔領格子上面符號則都不能使用
1.回合開始  第一回合外起始標記左傳，照順位可蓋把偵查隊轉成城市(偵查隊回手)，放置無城牆那面，如果該格有友軍移動到市郊，如果上回合有解鎖對應科技則可以改變政體，拿取對應面的政體卡放置圖板上
*蓋城市要符合城市最大值，起始2，有灌溉3，且該格不能是水域，市郊不能跟其他城市重疊或是在邊緣或是有茅屋跟村莊或是敵人單位
*如果不是在科技解鎖的當回合改變，則要先到無政府狀態，首都行動跳過也不會被打，下個回合開始階段在換到想要的政體
2.貿易階段 把所有城市市郊的貿易符號加總，轉對應格到貿易轉盤，之後所有玩家可彼此協商交易，可給予承諾，貿易值，文化標誌，資源指示物，文化事件卡等來交易
3.城市管理 照順位每位玩家的每座城市可以執行一個行動，一個玩家全部做完才換人
>生產或蓋建築  加總該城市市郊的生產符號，能利用此數量的生產符號生產或蓋小於等於需求的對應單位或建築(偵查隊的符號可選擇給一座城市使用) ，每三個貿易值可換成一個城市生產值
o軍隊4，偵查隊6，部隊卡根據等級支付費用(等級1為5，每高一級+2，飛行12)，隨機抽一張放到常備庫
o建築則要解鎖並蓋在市郊對應地形上(原本的符號被蓋掉不使用)
o有對應建築可蓋城牆，把一座城市標記翻面
o可選場上開著的一個奇觀來蓋，限制同建築(但是不算建築)，圖板抽一張新的奇觀補
*偵查隊不能進入村莊或茅屋，跟敵軍單獨同格直接移除，起始每格最多兩個同玩家單位
*被消滅的部隊卡面朝上放到對應牌庫底下，都蓋著都抽完洗勻在抽
*如果對應部隊有升級或建築有升級解鎖，則場上該建築都翻面，牌庫所有牌都視為該等級，之後要蓋都要支付該等級來蓋
*每個城市最多一座限定建築(有星號)跟一個奇蹟，所有建築物都可被其他取代
>奉獻藝術  拿取一個文明指示物，如果市郊有對應符合有幾個再拿幾個
*此階段隨時可支付對應數量個文明指示物來推文明軌，推多少都可以，當到達文化事件格抽對應事件卡，手牌上限2，可在對應時機棄掉使用，棄掉面朝上放在對應牌庫底，不夠抽重洗，重要人士格隨機抽重要人物標記，可馬上放城市市郊(如果被建築取代回手，下次回合開始可放)
>資源收穫  從城市市郊有看到的所有資源中選一個拿，用來發動科技卡能力
4.移動階段 每個場上偵查隊或士兵可根據移動速度移動對應格，起始2，可一群一起移動，一群上限起始2，有對科技才能穿越水域或停在上面，單位旁如果是位探索板塊，可花一點移動力翻開，箭頭面連結你單位在地該邊，放置對應的茅屋跟村莊蓋著
*士兵進入茅屋就拿回手，進入村莊下一個玩家代替村莊戰鬥，抽飛行器以外部隊卡各一(不夠抽從其他補足)，都視為一級，戰勝時取走村莊指示物，失敗則士兵移除
*移動時可通過自己城市板塊不能停留，進入敵方城市板塊則是為攻城，盡如有敵方士兵單位格則戰鬥
****戰鬥***
>雙方將部隊卡洗勻抽3張，士兵數第2個以上每隻在抽兩張，對應政體在抽一張，自己首都或城市在抽3張，使用對應該部隊等級拿面朝上
>計算戰鬥獎勵值，相減放對應數值的戰鬥獎勵牌在高的那方，每個軍營+2學院+4將軍+4城市+6，首都+12，有城牆+4
>守方開始輪流出一張牌(打有城牆時功方先出)，每張形成一個戰列，如果出在對方同列則戰鬥，攻擊值互相傷害，傷害超過其攻擊值的移除，沒超過的放對應受傷標記，傷害會累積，如果兵種相剋則先對對方造成傷害(步兵>騎兵>砲兵>)，沒移除才被打
>全部卡打完後，加總剩餘部隊的攻擊力跟戰鬥獎勵，高的贏，平手守方贏
>勝利方每兩個被消滅的部隊損使一個士兵(最少剩1)，失敗方全滅
>勝利者在非城市區域可拿走3貿易值或3文化指示物或任意資源(可暗抽對手的村莊或茅房)，在非首都城市則城市毀滅，所有建築過偉人移除，還可學會失敗方一個自己沒有的科技，偷對方一個文化事件卡，都兩個資源指示物等
>所有沒移除的部隊卡回常備庫
5.研發階段 根據等級1/2/3/4/5支付6/11/16/21/26的貿易值，且高等要比低等少1(形成金字塔)，一次只能研發一張，研發後只能保留等同經濟軌數量的貿易值
*升級士兵時將市場圖版的自己軍隊標記翻到對應面紀錄之
*要研發升級建築物科技，不需要已經研發未升級版本
*使用資源指示物的科技每輪只能使用一次
*錢幣指示物為某些科技卡完成任務時獲得，放上面同時轉經濟軌一格，地圖上有佔領錢幣格也會擁有，都也可能會失去

RATING: 9
This has turned out to be one of the favorites, but there are some things I'm not that big of a fan of, especially how the technologies have no prerequisite requirements apart from the levels, so it doesn't have the same kind of feel of developing a civilization rather than just develop something for your benefit like the other parts of the game. What I mean is you can get mass media without writing, for example. Then again, if this had some massive tech tree, it might become too difficult to follow with the amount of techs.

Another thing are the battles, that are slightly too abstract for my taste. It does not matter even if your units are on the other side of the planet, you still can defend your cities with your army. Technically I understand the idea of this. In a war, no major city is left undefended. Even if you have armies all over the world, there is some reserve staying in the cities. I like this aspect, but in the game it still is a tad too abstract. Battles itself are boring with the base game unit cards, but the revised cards from Wisdom&Warfare expansion makes them a lot better. 

This is one of the few games I keep thinking after every play how I could've played better.

Won from a giveaway by purplepawn.com

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An epic affair of building up your production engine, carefully buying exactly the right building for each city and putting it in the right place on the map, of carefully researching exactly the right technology ability to give you an edge over the other players ... and then it moves into the mid-game "who's winning, we better beat them up" phase, where armies get sent out to blokade and pummel. Therefore your aim is to first set up militarily strong enough to deter said mid-game beat-ups, and/or have the conviction and willingness to engage militarily to beat up anyone who's invested more in going for a faster win and has let their military lag as a result. The trick is being aware enough to know exactly what's going on in your opponents' worlds so you can act in time. The tech tree and building combinations are rich and need to be studied to enable the kind of insight needed to do well, not only for awareness, but in building smart combinations that will not only provide an edge but also a degree of satisfaction in developing a well-thought out strategy regardless of the inevitable kingmaker beat-ups that will likely happen in the end-game. While it won't come out a lot due to the time investment required, I'm sure I'll enjoy exploring the game further with each playing.

RATING: 5.5
An "explore, develop & growth" game should be concentrating on explore, develop & growth. This is ruined with warring. Warring is best suitable on wargames (which I do like a lot) but usually makes games like this less fun. The fastest way to win this game is by fighting, and if the others don't spend a lot of effort to stop the aggressive player there is NO other way to win the game before him. 

The game is also a bit short, especially if you take to account that it takes four hours to play. With short I mean you have very few turns & actions before the game ends. 

Added after my third try: 
Sadly this game gets worse for each play. Had to turn down my rating a notch. First time the win wasn't by warring, but it was due to warring. Three players killed off each other's military, and let the fourth win by collecting coins.

RATING: 8
Definitely the best medium length civ game out there. I like how well the resource handling is designed. There is no fiddling around with some resource tokens - you just add up the city´s production points and use them right away. The feel of city management and development is well represented. To me this game represents civ the computer game well enough - any more detailed and it becomes frustatingly fiddly (yes Antiquity, I am looking at you), any more abstract and it becomes dry or too light. Maybe the army system should have been different (more detailed and dice based perhaps?) but this would mean other parts of the game must be also designed a bit differently - and in this case maybe it wouldn´t be so great game anymore like it is now.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I really like the Civilisation computer game series. It isn't going to be easy recreating the experience of a solitaire computer game that lasts a month in a multiplayer board game that lasts an evening.
Unlike Through the Ages, Sid Meier's Civilization tries not to compromise the experience: there is a map, a tech tree, city development and some tactics in the military.
The result is a good multiplayer game with a feel for the computer game. I can accept the smaller map and the short tech tree to give a game of acceptable length.
On the downside, the three player map doesn't work well due to an edge of the map problem that limits city placement.
Also the military combat has problems - the game has to avoid a long combat sub game but I would prefer a simpler abstraction and do not like either the rock, scissors, paper approach, nor the fact that on a random card draw I can get a tank unit that can be defeated by a pikeman!
Not perfect but pretty good.
A friend has a copy and whilst I'm tempted to buy my own copy, I don't think that I'm going to.
Update: now that I own Clash of Cultures and Nations, this is completely off my purchase list - SMC:TBG needs the second expansion and probably the first as well and the result is too gimmicky and covered in band aids to compete.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Yuck.  Yuck yuck yuck.  

Tedious bookkeeping with itty-bitty counters and flimsy dials.  Really FFG?  Would some nice wooden cubes have been too much to ask?  I know that's not your style, but geez...

Too much randomness for a game of this length.  Not only are the strength of the units you buy random, but you don't even get to choose the units you take into battle.  You shuffle them up and draw at random, thus missing the opportunity to add a layer of strategy.  On top of that, exploring the map is random, not only in what's on the tile, but the orientation of said tile based on how you enter it.  You might wind up getting a great windfall, or you might get shafted.  Who knows!  Oh, and the culture cards are add yet another layer of randomness.  It may be just what you need, it may be crap.

Deterministic strategies based on which civ you start with.  So, each civ has a different ability.  Okay.  Oh, but if you don't focus on a strategy that plays to your civ's strength, you're going to handicap yourself?  Way to limit a player's choices even more.

Another solid disappointment from Fantasy Flight Games.

RATING: 7
This is a very unique in depth strategy board game. The rules can take a while to get to know, but the rule book does a fantastic job at explaining them. Civilization The Board Game supports up to four players, and it the game can take between 2-3 hours with two people and 3-4 hours with four people. This is a game that requires a lot of thought and strategy to play. A person can win in four ways: technology, culture, economy, and military. Depending on which victory a person chooses to pursue it can completely change the person's strategy. Another important quality this game has is that there are no dice, and hardly any luck.

The first time you open the box to this game you will be amazed at the amount of color and artistic images that are included. There are over hundred tokens, and the civilization cards all have a great drawing of a famous leader belonging to each civilization. This game has over 10 map tiles that each have 9 squares of different land types that look very artistic and beautiful. Images are also included on all of the military cards to represent all of the unit types including infantry, artillery, calvary, and aircraft. Each of these things may not make up entirely for the great graphics and animations you will find in the civilization video game, but they do a pretty good at transferring the video game's visuals into a board game.

The only things I would say are bad about this game is how long it takes to set up and play.

RATING: 5
Love the idea of a vast civilization building game.  The actual implementation is a little clunky.  In research, it's a definite race.  If you fall behind at any point -- you take longer than others to establish a second/third city, they use an income booster to put them over the requirement for the next tier a turn earlier than you -- you've lost the race, and no matter how good you do after that, it is very difficult to get back in it.  Sabotage or warfare are your realistic options.
The combat system... I don't like it.  There are four types of units, and the three basic types are essentially rock-paper-sissors setup.  You build a deck of combat cards by purchasing a random card of a certain type (which can be upgraded within its type by technology).  Each card will be one of three rankings; in the beginning this means a good card is three times more powerful than a bad one.  The aforementioned unit upgrades simply add one to the power of a given card, so a good level 1 card is just as numerically powerful as a bad level 3.  Combat is run by shuffling and dealing a number of cards to be your hand, the number based on how many armies you're fielding in the battle.  Most of the time, to me, it feels like just rolling dice would be equally as effective and a lot more fair for managing combat.

All that said, the varied technologies and different approaches to building schemes, not to mention the flavor of the game, attract me to keep giving it just one more try.

RATING: 4
UPDATE: Second game, 4 player game, two days after Christmas. The rules are horribly written; this is a very simple game to learn and teach. Our second game went so much faster. Maybe only 2-2.5 hours?.

[b]This is not a negotiation game.[/b]

Dramatically downgraded from a 7 to a 4. I think I initially rated it high because I was enjoying copious amounts of beer.

Playing it sober make me realize that this game is a typical cube-pushing. economic-engine Euro. I'm actually willing to concede that this could be a well-designed Euro. Of course, it should be considered Ameritrash, which is just like any other pretentious Euro except for the following:

* Garish components -- artwork on individual tiles and cards is usually excellent, but the game as a whole is sight-numbing.

* Tiny die-cut cardboard chits in place of cubes

* Really, really, really fiddly and lots and lots of special power cards, specifically for recovering Magic the Gathering addicts.

* Direct conflict in place of some gimmicky mechanic to add some trace of player interaction. Some explanation here: fights between players occur sparingly but usually swing the balance of the game; losing a battle is disastrous. In addition, it's surprisingly easy to determine who will win the battle, provided that you have a good memory of your opponent's battle cards and have enough battle cards. So what happens is that if there's a warmonger among the players, everyone else has two strategies to balance: building up forces or racing to victory. This is all quite good: the player interaction is all about anticipating what you're opponents plan to do, and react accordingly. However, the focus is on this race to victory -- the race to build the VP engine the fastest -- and the military pressures each player faces simply adds a wrench to the proceedings.

As I mentioned before, I really don't like VP engine games, and the focus on Civ is this race to build the most efficient VP engine. I prefer games in which players have to focus on manipulating their opponents rather than the game's system.

=====================

One play during Christmas 2010, three player game.

I'm not a big fan of "4X" games (explore, expand, exploit, and exterminate, according to Wikipedia), but this one seems to be well designed.

What made this game work, for me, is how the tech tree is implemented:

1. All technologies are available to all players
2, It is impossible for a player to build all technologies; this forces specialization and more difficult strategic decisions
3. Building higher tech levels does not require excessive cross-referencing; you don't need a specific technology to build a particular tech.

Still, it's a Fantasy Flight game, and thus a sterling example of Ameritrash, which, depending on your tastes, is either a godsend or a scourge:

1. Awfully gaudy artwork: Individually, each tile is decently illustrated with lots of detail. However, as a whole, it's a eye-straining mess. Couple that with all the tiny icons all over the board, and small text describing a special power, first games will definitely be learning games, or even off-putting for casual gamers. For instance, in our first game, we didn't bother with building wonders (which is a very valid tactic) because they were simply too far away from us; we were too busy going through our technology decks to bother.

2. Multiple paths to victory, but each path is a straight line: This is a common feature I find in many Euro games: there are about four main ways to win: build fifteen technologies, conquer a player's capital city, reach the end of the culture track (which involves spending culture points, one of many commodities in the game), and earning 15 coins. About halfway through the game, players will recognize the most efficient way to achieve each of these goals. I could compare this to Euro economic engine games in which you need one thing to get this, which gets you this, which gets you something else, and eventually a victory point. The trick in this game is to find the balance between achieving your goal and hindering your opponents. This, of course adds a lot of tension -- and clarity since players know exactly how to achieve these goals, and I believe many players to enjoy this. However, I have a preference for games with simple winning conditions, but they way to get there is not at all straightforward or intuitive.

3. Long and fiddly. The varying quality of the components does not help either. The tiles are made of very thick cardboard. However the card stock of the playing cards are cheap; I find it annoying that Fantasy Flight expects its customers to by sleeves -- they even advertise on the box which size of sleeves you should purchase.

4. A lot of special power cards. Lots. Players have 36 technologies to choose from, about eight different forms of government to pick from (which gives a player a special power), about ten different kinds of buildings and 6 "great people" (most of them simply give a player one or more resources), and about twenty different "wonders" to be built. I know many players love this kind of variety. As I mentioned before, I like how all players have access to all technologies, unlike games like Le Havre in which the player interactions is the competition for these special powers. Personally, though, I prefer games with elegant rules and not many of these special powers.

RATING: 4
Disclaimer: rated as a 2-player game!

Longish civilization builder without many significant decisions. At first it may feel overwhelming (especially the lenghty rulebook), but when you actually start to understand the various aspects of the game, the paths to victory seem rather obvious. While there are numerous technologies to choose from, you lack almost any flexibility - some are just superior for the choosen path no matter how you look at it.

The military system is rather odd and very random; having some weaker units in your standing forces makes you wish to draw them during the easier fights to deliberately kill them and get rid of them (that's almost like a deckbuidling minigame, only it's not interesting at all).

Moreover, if, during a 2-player game, both players go for a peaceful approach, it's just a stale, boring solitaire. Absolutelly nothing happens, so, if you want at least a bit of tension, you should invest in your army to kick some butt. 

Long story short, I didn't like it that much.

SLIGHTLY CLUNKY BUT VERY FUN 4X GAME
Finally got around to playing this twice around TG weekend 2011 (approx. 11 months after acquisition (its like that sometimes)). Two (2P) plays in I understand generally how to play but feel like I have merely nicked the surface of a very deep game.  There is a lot to pay attention to, too much for the first couple of plays.  Definitely an all day game, at least with inexperienced players.  I think you need to commit to your strategy early in this game - switching gears against a competent opponent is a recipe for a loss.  Definitely a GAMER'S GAME - but the casual player should stay away.

Third play - still excellent.  This game REALLY does give the Civilization Building Experience in a manageable package.  I totally feel the theme while playing this game, unlike say Through the Ages which is very clever but feels more like an exercise in moving round disks around.  I love the modular map.

RATING: 6
This game is such a typical Fantasy Flight product. There are loads of bits, all well produced, and it is based on a classic game (in this case a computer game). The turn is simpler than it initially appears - collect trade/take one action with each city (you have up to three)/move armies and conduct battles/use that trade to buy technologies. 
Everything fits around that basic structure. There are four different ways to win and while you have to work towards one it doesn't feel like you are totally locked into it. The whole thing comes down to a race at the end which can be quite close.
There are some down-sides. There are a lot of pieces, it is clearly quite easy to end up off the pace, and there is a lot of down time if a player is not involved in the game. And it does take a fair bit of time.
The computer game of course borrows a title from a 1980s game which is real classic and this doesn't really live up to the elegance and simplicity of that. Still if you want a slightly meatier game and don't fancy a space setting this is a worth while game.

RATING: 6
My initial high hopes were somewhat disappointed, but with more plays the game revealed itself to be more interesting than at first sight. It certainly seems that economic victories are too easy and culture victories too difficult (a fact clearly acknowledged in the tweaks in the expansion). I can't escape the sneaking suspicion that FFG brought the game out a tick before the design was fully matured; it really needs the expansion to be complete.

The game is all about efficiency, and making an early mistake can cost you victory. You need a good idea from  very early on of which of the paths to victory you will use. It is possible to interfere with the other player's plans by military attacks and using culture cards, and knowing which player should be kept in check is an essential skill for an enjoyable game: the players balance out the game, not the mechanics. 

To sum up, while I'm overcoming my initial scepticism, and will certainly play it if it is suggested, there are still a few things I'm not overly enamoured of: I dislike the combat system as being overly deterministic and taking far too long when one considers that the result is rarely in doubt at the beginning; the game's feel of an efficiency race is not much to my taste; at the end of the game, turn order can be of supreme importance.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I've owned this game since it was released. I'm one of the few who actually like Eagle Games' old translation of the computer game franchise, and was eagerly looking forward to FFG's take on it.

I had the box lying around gathering dust for about a year, while trying to get it played at least three times. For some reason, the rule book killed my enthusiasm every time. I don't abhor rules, generally - I like a meaty, complex game - but something about these 30 pages of not very exceptional instructions just put me off, and convinced me to pick up another title instead.

The fourth time, I almost didn't read it through (again). Setup was slightly confusing, as rules are cross referenced from paragraph to paragraph, and you have to scrutinize the rather small image of how the board is supposed to look like rather closely to get all the pieces in the right places.

Likewise, the first turn or two was chaos. Battle incomprehensible, in its simplicity, and resource management baffling. Then, something clicked and the game took off!

This is exactly the kind of game I like. Multiple victory conditions, lots of important choices both short term and in the long run - you don't have the time to do everything you'd like (a plus, in my book), and even though it seems you have to follow one singular strategy to win you have to keep investing at least a little something in the other victory avenues too - to keep pressure on other players who are pursuing them.

Summa summarum: For some reason, this game has a steeper learning curve than it should, for a relatively simple game. Once you get into it, it's easy to play, with great depth, variety and replay value.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
I love this game.  It's FFG produced, so you know all the MANY components in this game are well made and fit their purpose.  There is a ton going on in this game to keep you thinking, and while tense, I always have fun.  Even when I lose horribly, I just enjoy playing this game.  Downtime between turns is cut down by some of the phases being simultaneous, but there is still some downtime when playing with AP prone players.  My biggest qualms are that my game group can't play this in under four hours and I had to remove the beautiful insert to fit all the pieces back in the box.  I guess you can't have it all, but this game comes very close.

RATING: 6
Epitome of bad elements in FFG design style: wanting to fit everything from the computer counterpart resulted in lots of clunky mechanisms which don't fit together that well, many of which seem a bit out-of-place in a civ sim game: rock-paper-scissors battle resolution, take-that element in the form of culture cards, which can do random bad things to opponents by flicking your fingers and more. RATING: 8
Could they not have given this a substantially different name than the previous incarnation - "Sid Meier's Civilization: The Boardgame". Would that have been so hard? Perhaps "Sid Meier's Civilization: Revolution", or "Sid Meier's Civilization: déjà vu".

At first, despite the good attempt to simplify the game into a board game ultimately it's still a fiddly beast. But then, after a few plays you realise it's not really - it plays rather smoothly. And what a nice looking and entertaining beast it is. It's seen multiple plays in my house and each time I marvel more and more at how clever the combat system is, how beautifully balanced the whole beast is and how much fun we have with it.

My only concern is that the playing style always favours an aggressive and militaristic strategy - certainly it's a safer path to follow. That kind of reflects the computer version though - gamers tend to like to beat each other up. It's no more militaristic than Civilization: Through the Ages and there's something satisfying about having bits to move around a board.

A good effort that I see has a continued place in my gaming cupboard. With games running in at easily under three hours it's a keeper for me.

RATING: N/A
Civilization has some cool stuff going on. The different paths to victory all seem legitimate. The tech tree implementation is simple, but still allows for a lot of customization. I like the cultural progression, and the different cards and great people that provide extra abilities.

But I don't really like this game in general. It doesn't move along very quickly since there is obviously a lot to think about. Combat during the game is kind of lame; there aren't a lot of meaningful military decisions. And counting up trade and production gets kind of messy.

I can definitely see why a lot of people would like this game; the progression is noticeable and significant, and the nation growth could be exciting. But I thought it just kind of lumbered along. Maybe 4X games aren't for me? I'm not sure.
