RATING: 7
I like Civilization in a lot of ways.  I like having a map, exploration, expanding technologies.  Also, a lot of the game is fairly intuitive.  There are some things I am not crazy about.  

It seems to me that the military could be better.  For one thing the military battles seem too punitive and are a bit unbalanced.  The losing player loses (1) a figure, (2) all his cards in play, and (3) a resource or some other spoil.  The losing player would have to spend at least 4 civil actions, maybe more, to even get back to where he started and the other player gets ahead.  Second, I think the mechanics lead to an odd situation that if a player is weak, his military figures become a liability.  I am not sure if the rules permit it, but there are cases when it would be desirable to just disband or destroy ones own military figures.  If not, a stronger player can attack to obtain spoils.  It being a benefit to destroy one’s own army figures seems really odd, but if you are weak, then having no military figures is the best way to protect your resources.  That seems really counterintuitive.  In a related though, it also seems counterintuitive to me that invading armies can only obtain spoils by finding an enemy military figure and defeating it.  It seems like invading armies should benefit more from ravaging unchallenged parts of the enemy’s territory.    Instead of seeking needed resources, a military figure invades looking for other military figures rather than resources or the like and in fact cannot obtain spoils unless that happens.  Just seems odd to me.

The paths to victory seem unbalanced and that is a bit of a disappointment.  The tech tree seems overweighted.  Purchasing techs requires only one currency, trade.  Trade is fairly easy to accumulate, as you aggregate it from all your cities and it takes no action to harvest it.   If you get the right buildings in place it is not too hard to get a high trade value.  And then the purchasing of techs requires no civil action.  So, it is a powerful combination, you can improve your technology (and get closer to victory) for no civil actions.  It is pretty easy to churn out techs, especially considering the presence of events and abilities like Exchange of Ideas.

I think the culture track could be better.  I think it is too long and expensive to make it a comparable victory condition to the tech tree, or possibly even the military victory (I am not as sure about that).  Moving along the culture takes a lot of actions.  Getting culture is pretty hard and usually resource intensive.  You generally need to either harvest culture directly from a city by using a city action or otherwise need to acquire a good to run through some ability that converts it to culture.  Harvesting from a city takes the city’s whole action and the harvest applies only to that single city (unlike harvesting trade, which is aggregated from all cities).  Further, in the second and third ages, moving along the culture track takes more culture and other resources as well, which makes it even more expensive.  Also, a lot of the culture events are not that great.  Some are ok, but many are not that useful and not enough to compensate for the cost.  

Maybe civilization is a little but like Caylus.  In Caylus, one of the favor tracks is significantly stronger than the others.  Perhaps the tech tree is the strongest way to go and a strong military is essential.  But the advertising of the game was that each path was viable.  Perhaps it is in some games.  I can envision games where no players are militarily aggressive and each person just does his own thing.  In those cases I would think the tech tree would still be the easiest path to victory, but culture might have a bit more of a shot, depending on one’s map.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I love the way the tech tree works in this game.

The combat is okay, but I don't like how random it can be in the early game, and it can be difficult to replace units in your standing forces. If one player has a disadvantage in production, another player can strip them of all their units by causing battles until they run out, then just take out their capital. They won't be able to replenish their units fast enough without 2-3 high production cities and engineering. Overall, I prefer other combat systems.

I have 2 main issues with this game; the balance between the different victory paths is a bit wonky with just the base game. Finance and military appear to be the only viable victory conditions without the expansion. As soon as someone gets a decent military advantage, they can just win. Otherwise, a financial victory seems to be ~3-5 turns faster than a tech victory, and also faster than a cultural victory. The expansion helps with this issue.

The other issue I have with the game is how much it forces you to specialize in order to win. I prefer games where you can take a more dynamic approach to the strategy. This works well with VP systems, but not with a game like Civ, that has 4 different victory conditions. If you try to do everything, and someone else specializes, they will win unless you kill them first.

Kíváncsi voltam a Through the Ages után milyen lesz a Civilization. Féltem, hogy sokkal gyengébb, de kellemesen csalódtam. Kellemes játék a Civilization, élvezetes két személyesen is. Kezdjük a negatívumokkal. Hosszú és talán túl sok a lehetőség is. A hosszúság persze relatív és egy ilyen témával foglalkozó játéknál meg is engedhető, de úgy érzem 4 játékosnál nagyon sokáig tarthat egy parti és a királycsinálás lehetősége bőven adott. A túl sok lehetőség pedig talán kicsit csökkenthető lett volna. Pl. az első játék alkalmával mi csodát nem is építettünk. Épület is túl sok van szerintem, de ez mindenképpen növeli a változatosságát a játéknak. És akkor jöjjenek a pozitívumok. 4 fajta nyerési út, amiből talán egy túl nehéz a többihez képest, de sokfajta stratégiai út járható. Minden játék más, jó a változatos térképelemek felfedezése, nagyon jó a harcrendszer, szinte önálló játék a játékban. Érdekesek a fejlesztések, jó a piramisos ötlet és nyerési út. A jó stratégia a dinamikus fejlődés mindenben, lehet egy ilyetén pontozásvariáns még jobbá tehetné a játékot. Jó játék a Civilization, a TTA dinamikus változásait nem éri el, de megállja a helyét, szívesen játszanék még vele.

RATING: 6.5
1 play, 4er.

Seems to do a pretty good job of translating the computer game into board game form, in a very different way than Through the Ages. This does have more of a tech tree than TTA, but it's not exactly a tree but more like a pyramid (no specific prerequisites; just need 2 techs of a lower level). Each player also starts with a randomly assigned starting civ, each with its own advantage. The board is also randomly set up and revealed as you explore. Both these things, esp the starting civ, seem to highly dictate your options.

It seems like I ought to like the game more -- and I'll likely play it at least once more -- but I think I'd almost always rather just play Through the Ages!

RATING: 7
This was the first civ builder board game I've played.  It is basically a watered down version of the video game with a lot of the same theme and mechanics.  In fact just reading the rulebook put me in the mood to play Civ V and I've put between 8-10 hours into that game over the past week alone.  Just one more turn!

So my experience was more good than bad.  First the good.  I love the variable factions, technology tree, component variety, modular map setup, and different paths to victory.  All of this makes for a varied, very replayable game with lots of interesting choices and branching decision trees.  The social interaction is a huge boon as well, I love negotiating, scheming, threatening, and pleading with my opponents across the table.  It's also quite thematic as each faction has certain specialties and you really feel yourself improving over time as you build more advanced buildings and gain new technology and abilities.

But there was a bit of bad too.  First of all the game is long and my first play was made even longer (approaching 5 hours!) by players asking too many questions and taking too much time to take a handful of actions (especially one player in particular).  It's funny because I don't balk at spending 5+ hours playing video games and RPGs but I've figured out that's because I'm constantly doing something.  That's 5+ hours of pretty constant activity.  In this game I timed it and there were instances where I alone was waiting 30 minutes just for my next action which took a couple of seconds.  30 minutes of doing nothing is brutal and when it happens every round it wears on you.

A few of my other complaints involve parts of the system that can't really be changed.  For one there appears to be balance issues, not necessarily with the different factions (though there may be, it wasn't apparent in one play) but one of the most interesting parts of the game is the different victory conditions but they do not appear to be created equal.  Culture in particular seems very difficult to achieve and a military victory seems a little bit easier than the others.  Starting with a military focused faction would give you even more of a head-start.  And while other players can garrison to prevent this that also distracts from them achieving their own victory.  Again though, these are observations from a single play.  Also, I wish the map and movement rules were designed so that in a 4 player game all civilizations were equidistant but each player always has one opponent they are further from than the others due to the square setup and orthogonal movement but this is a minor gripe.  My biggest issue with the game is I quite dislike the combat system.  It is uninteresting, overly convoluted, and kind of stupid.  It doesn't really mimic the video game so it's not an issue of a poor attempt to translate that and with so many tried and true war games to borrow the card- or dice-driven combat systems from I think this was just a failed attempt to do something a little original.

If I ever played this game again, it would have to be under 3 conditions:  1.  This game has a ton of little fiddly components, it should be set up on the table before everyone arrives.  It's one thing if you're not sure you're going to play a game or not but this is something you plan an evening around.  That alone can cut 20+ minutes out of total play time.  2.  Everyone needs to read and, this is the important part, UNDERSTAND the rulebook before you play.  Yes, it is a deep system and yes, some stuff you can only really fully grasp after you play and you will inevitably be asking questions and flipping back through the book.  But my first play had some people asking questions (and sometimes the SAME questions over and over) about very simple concepts in the system that proved they either didn't read the rulebook or didn't understand what they were reading before playing.  3.  All players need to make a concerted effort to have their moves planned out before it is their turn.  Yes, there will be occasions where you need to slow down and consider your options, especially toward the end of the game.  But in a four player game you have 3 other player's turns to have at least made an effort to work out your strategy (not to mention I assume you've spent the whole rest of the game at least working on a guideline of what you're attempting to do) and there is no excuse for 30 minutes of downtime for a player for just one phase of one turn.  There also needs to be a player who understands the game well-enough and has a leadership mentality enforcing these guidelines, especially when it comes to telling players to "Go!"
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

sur les tablettes de l'Espace Sociojeux.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A

It's kind of a win win situation. On the other hand, military and cultural victories are not that easy to achieve and I think that if not for the kick of it no player will try to achieve any of them. This is my first and not so solid opinion about the game. I've only played it once!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Any board game of Sid Meier's Civ. is going to be a must have for me (apart from Eagle's effort :)) 

It's designed by Kevin Wilson (Arkham, Android and Descent) - and I'm hoping that he will bring some magic to this.

And he has! Now played and it feels like Civ. Relatively straightforward rules but huge complexity in the paths to victory and strategies. This has the loads of tokens n cards common to many FF Games, and as such there is a bit of a house style feel to the game. When the house style is applied to some Fantasy setting I don't give two hoots about (the Nurgle, the Snurgggle ,the...whatever) it makes the game difficult to follow because the differences between racial powers, techs etc etc are not intuitive. In Civ the house style works because the powers and techs are intuitive and logical - we 'know' the Russians like stealing technology and the Chines are good at building walls - and we know from years of playing on the Pc game that Pottery inlocks Granaries. Explaining the game to Civ fans is relatively easy, much less so to those not familiar with the Pc Game. The only concepts that the Civ player might go 'What' to are the culture track and the notion of standing forces but command and control armies that wander the map.

I think the game needs to be played aggressively, using your armies to either lay in to the opposition or demand tribute in the trade phase.  However, four pacifists could ignore each other and play the optimisation route. There is a bit of beauty in the game that you can play the game in a style that suits you. I think to play optimally though you need to plan your strategy from the beginning around the Civ. you start with and the resources available in your first 2 cities. 

5th game not so great - Germans, won but multiplayer military issues

6th player sans the Hun, Great game first time i have seen a coin victory

7th with expansion - v quick Greek military victory, level 4 techs ultra fast

8th after a long break...Played with both expansions. Enjoyed playing. However, social policies and Investments just gild the lily - they add a layer of specialisation that I don't enjoy - too hard to see what others are doing, and add focuses the player back on their tableau.

I do like the great leader cards, the new combat, city states and new techs.

RATING: 6
This is a good game that disappointed me.  Finally, we had a full-scale, map-based civ game that could be played in a few (okay, maybe 4; 5 at the very most) hours.  Players could bring their unique civilization from the dawn of humanity right through to space flight, while developing their economic, cultural, and military might.  Even the artwork is pretty decent (even if the map army tokens are incredibly boring flag tokens).  And I'll also give props for attempting a unique combat system, although it ends up being a needlessly time-consuming, rock-paper-scissors based model.  However, the game also comes chock-full of the typical Fantasy Flight fiddliness: tons of cards and little cardboard tokens, a zillion little rules to remember, and have I mentioned the fiddly combat system?  But most unforgivably of all, and what ultimately ruins the game for me, is that players are forced to channel themselves down a specific victory path rather than winning based upon building a diverse civilization.  So while this game is still a good option for people who are willing to deal with its quirks and complexities and victory conditions, for my tastes, now that I have Clash of Cultures, I do not feel the need to ever play this game again.  Game design: 3.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 2.5/5  Total: 6/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow this is a fun game.  It does an excellent job of simulating the feel of the Civilization computer game without bogging down in complex minutia of city management and having tons of different unit types.  In other words I think the game is very good at abstracting the elements of the computer game that really are not necessary and retaining the elements that make the game fun.  I think it is brilliant how they implemented building up technologies with the tech "pyramid" system.  That obviates the need for any kind of complicated tech "map" like the computer game has.  Game play is simple after a couple rounds and you do get the feel (somewhat) that you are building up a civilization over time.  The only minor gripes I have are: 1) The game is somewhat fiddly with a lot of counters which creates a long set up time 2) Game play can take a long time, particularly with newer players 3) I get the sense you must choose an initial victory condition to pursue and then you cannot deviate from it or you risk spreading yourself too thin and no longer being competitive.  In other words, I don't see this as a game where you can easily make a mid-game course change and still reasonably expect to win.  I suppose real civilizations are like that, however, it doesn't make the game play as enjoyable/strategic as I would like it to be.  This criticism may not be entirely correct, however, because I have only played the game once.  Further plays will help me flesh out this issue more fully.  Still, I really enjoyed playing the game and look forward to future plays.

Basato sull’acclamato video game Civilization progettato da Sid Meier, allo stesso modo Civilization: Edizione Italiana pone i giocatori al comando di un fiorente impero. Attraverso la programmazione strategica e scelte oculate i giocatori possono modellare la civiltà dei loro sogni.

Potete costruire l’esercito più potente della storia e dominare i vostri avversari? O vi focalizzerete nel progredire la vostra tecnologia per riuscire, un giorno, a viaggiare tra le stelle? • Un eccitante tabellone di gioco ispirato alla classica serie di video giochi Sid Meier’s Civilization

• Partendo da umili origini, è possibile condurre sei differenti civiltà storiche alla grandezza e prosperità

Generally, I have a feeling that the game offers nothing new, except of some mechanic elements that are rather mediocre.

RATING: 9
I was a big fan of Through the Ages, but for me, this game improves on that game in many ways. It is more streamlined, easier to teach, has less AP, and you don't have to wait minutes for your turn.

Yes, Through the Ages probably has deeper game play, but this version of Civ has a lot of options in it. With 4 ways to win it gives you a lot of variety.

With the inclusion of a map and culture cards, plus limited buildings, Civ also gives more interaction between the players.

It is abstract in some ways, but not as much as Through the Ages. I also feel that this game is closer to the PC game than Through the Ages also, except that Through the Ages makes you balance your resources more.

Right now, I think this is the best Civ game I've played. This may change if I ever get to play the original game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I played many different board games in my life, and most of then I played a lot. But I have never played a game as good as Sid Meier's Civilization: The Board Game. First of all, the box and the components are awesome. The quality of all tokens, map tiles, figures and cards is excelent. Even the manual has a high quality standard. For the game price, it's crazy. Also, the game is gorgeus. It's impossible to stay impassible to the board when the game is set up, even my wife that understand nothing from these games spend some time just looking to the table when the game is there.

But the best part is yet to come. The game is absolutely awesome. I read most of the negative comments here, and clearly most people played the game 1 time (some even less!). 1 user gave a bad rate and said that the combat should be decided in a throw of the dice... crazy.

The rules are detailed, but very intuitive. Of course, you'll need to play the game 3-5 times to play it perfectly without looking the manual once, until you get the hang of it. But which complex game isn't like this? Basically, the turn is divided in 5 steps:

1 - Build new cities and/or change government;
2 - Collect trade;
3 - City Action;
4 - Movement;
5 - Research tech.

The four paths to victory are balanced well enough, but this will be VERY affected by the way your group plays. If your players always go for economic wins, for example, you may think that the economic win is overpowered and unbalanced... but this is not the case. Just check the forum, there are groups complaining that tech, militar, culture and economic victories are overpowered... if all are overpowered, none is.

The combat of the game is simple, but strategic. There is little randomness involved, so you must be prepared to attack and to defend. There is surprise enough with the randomness involved (size and power of your army) and with the culture cards, there is also surprise enough. This, with the tech resource abilities, make the battle very pleasant and thrilling.

The game is played between 150 to 180 minutes. The game is 80% played simultaneously by all players, so it does not get boring and you are always thinking and planning.

In general, there is not much randomness involved in the game, which is great. I don't to play a 3 hour strategic game that in the end was decided by luck.

Just get it and play. I haven't played a better game in my life.

RATING: 6
Surprisingly entertaining. I say surprising because heavily thematic Fantasy Flight games are not typically to my liking.

Civilization is probably too long, and the tech trees add complications that are going to take you a while to learn, but the game played smoothly. With multiple paths to victory, you can choose to focus your particular civilization on a handful of areas. For example, in my first game, I one by building up my military faster than the other players. In my second game, I won again (not that I'm showing off!) by building up my tech tree. You'll have to focus on something, so the game encourages you to try different strategies from game to game. I question how flexible you can be if, for example, you see that another player is concentrating on the same area, but at least it's nice to feel that there is real variety.

Overall, I also like the battle system. It gives a huge advantage to the attacker (which is a nice change from most games), but is brisk and tactical without being overly luck based. Each player has a hand of cards, which the play one at a time in a simplified maneuver/matching game. I'm not fully enamored with the card play as it feel like a mini game within a game that can be either distracting or else obvious without adding much strategy. However, it's unique and does not overstay its welcome.

On the other hand, I will say that battles are very decisive -- maybe too much, or at least so much so that military action is a better deterrent than viable strategy unless you are willing to go all in. You simply have to engage in an arms race if for no other reason than to protect what you've built. So while the battle system is overall interesting, the military aspect of the game is a touch too powerful.

Civilization then is a good game, certainly worth a handful of plays. With a game like Scythe available now, I'm not sure Civilization has the luster it once had, but if you're the type of person who wants a bigger tech tree and a larger focus on military battles, then this may be the game for you.

RATING: 9.5
This game "insta-wowed" me. The only concern I might have is that it is long. BUT - it never felt like it was. Not a single second, in which you didn't seem involved or you weren't interested in what was happening over at your opponent's civilization. There's just so much going on all the time. Interactivity is at an all-time high including trade negotiations and tons of "Kettenrasseln", which would be best translated by cold war conduct. But it differs from i.e. TI 3rd edition, in that it does not remain cold through most of the game, but is bound to happen quite often without the big initial bang that everyone is lurking for and then starts, but you have to be agressive and then again mild with your oppenents throughout all the game. 

And then there's the myriad of points, Kevin Wilson perfectly converted from the computer game to the board game:
- The tech trees
- stealing techs (althoughthat's just the Russians)
- the Wonders
- the adjustment of the cities as towards what their production shall be focussed on
- the victory conditions (Technology victory, Trade victory, Culture victory, military victory)
- the personalities
- the resources
.
.
.

Plus a combat system that is both unique and feliticious.
 
I could rave about how great this game is and the fantastic initial impressions it left me with and that I still didn't put to words as I'm still not done recalling them all, when really everything I'd love to do is to play it again. If it is civilization-building you want, this is THE place to go.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
+ Comes with all the trappings of the video games. Familiar buildings, wonders, great people, etc.

+ The tech pyramid is super cool. The techs get extremely powerful at the top, but you only get to use a couple of them each game, which leaves you wanting to try the others out.

Nice, distinct powers for each of the civilizations give them their own flavor; however, this can lead to feeling a bit rail-roaded towards a particular strategy.

- The battle system is a step above rolling dice but still a mess. Huge, randomly determined power swings between units. Interesting rock/paper/scissors system but not enough cards (randomly drawn each combat) to do anything but add some additional luck.

- Extremely theme first design. The game is a mess of a mechanics with little connection between them. This manifests itself as a lot of little rules to explain delivered via a poor rulebook.

- Squares instead of the standard hexes. Presumably another theme first design choice, given that hexes didn't make it to the video games until the fifth entry.

A pretty poorly designed game hidden behind a great license. After a couple of plays, the novelty of the license wore off and the mechanics don't stand up. Traded away.

RATING: 9
I love nearly every aspect of this game.  Being a long-time fan of the video game series probably doesn't hurt, but this game easily stands on its own merits.  My favourite part is that this is one of the few strategy games with asymmetric factions that REALLY makes each faction feel completely different.  Surely, one of them is overpowered - but WHICH one, they all seem overpowered!!  Sure, the victory conditions are not terribly well-balanced - for example, an economic win is FAR more viable than a Culture win - but to me, the hallmark of a good game is one where I discover MORE options every time I play.  So far, this has invariably been true of Civilization.

RATING: 7
Rating Without Wisdom and Warfare expansion: 5

Epic, civbuilding game. The sense of completion/progress you get as you proceed through this game is great, as are most of your ingame decisions. The starting civs do make a difference in your strategies, and the level of choices you have at every level is staggering in a good way. I think that with the expansions, this game is the best civilization game with a map, easily.

I can't see how this can be finished in under 3-4 hours, unless of course you have a highly trained (and not prone to analysis paralysis) group. It's too much for two players, good with 4, even better with 3. Although luck is not an option, the game is somewhat prone to kingmaking, though less so with experienced players. The ways to victory also seem unbalanced, unless someone has both expansions apparently.

Overall this is a great modern game, provided of course that you include at least wisdom and warfare. Without it, the ridiculous randomness of the armies and the unbalance of the victory conditions ruin for me the game almost completely.

RATING: 7.5

**UPDATE**

This is a great game but in true FFG style there are many rules inconsistencies that tarnish an otherwise excellent game. I've had two games where I've been able to win that turn but because of turn order I've been denied a win. The tie-breaker variant in the first official FAQ solves this somewhat, but there are a load more questions that need to be answered. Hopefully Civ gets an Arkham Horror style rewrite. At this point- it needs it.

Having said that though, I still love the game and am prepared to put up with minor inconsistencies. If the major ones were fixed, that would be great.

RATING: 8.5
[i]Sid Meier's Civilization: The Board Game[/i] captures the spirit of the Civilization computer games, even if it is a little light in some parts. The game should not be considered competition to deep, in-depth civilization building games, as the game is much shorts and lighter than many of that kind. However, it is a fully-accessible and fun game that can be played in three hours. Deep micromanagers will not have that itch satisfied by this game, but those who like map exploration and city building portions of empire building games will find that this game will charm them with a fun experience.

Full review at: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/581761/

RATING: 9
What I like: 

-This game is so epic. It's so goddamn satisfying advancing your civilization in this - so many upgrades and the way you see your empire expand on the actual board instead of just in an abstract manner - It's so satisfying. 
-4 completely unique paths to victory makes this so interesting. Also like that each civilization has their own unique features. 

What I dislike:
-The plaaaaaaaaaying time! Oh my, this game just takes forever. I do NOT recommend playing this without someone at the table knowing the rules perfectly - if you have to look up stuff during your plays this might take you all day
-The AP is so ridiculously strong in this one - The thing is, since you know everything that is and will be available to you, you can literally plan out your entire game! And you kinda have to! And don't you dare stray from your original strategy - if you planned to go for culture or gold or science you better darn well stay on that path, cause otherwise you've just declared yourself completely lost. The progress you made in one field does not translate to others (which makes sense), so you can't really switch strategy mid-game. This is the kind of game where you could easily plan the entire game out before you even started playing. 
-Since expanding with more cities is so important, you can get screwed abit if you draw poor tiles early game to get your extra cities in.

RATING: 3.5
This was brutal to learn and try to play without forgetting stuff. And I don't see anything of interest to the game.

What drew me to the game (aside from liking the earlier Civ computer games) was the modular board. The size scales by number of players, but in a 4p, there are 16 boards in play, four of which start revealed, and up to eight more of which might be revealed halfway through the first turn. Such a fast rate of exploration of the world feels quite unlike Civ on a computer.

Aside from the modular board, you have a standardish multiplayer conquest game and some standardish not overly complicated economy building. Player interaction is mostly in the former component and there isn't much subtlety to it. There are four ways to win: One is by conquering another player's capital, and the other three all involve using your economy to work your way up what are essentially just tracks (culture, economic, or technology). Nothing promising about any of this, just lots of tedious economic bonuses to remember and an asinine combat system (a card system that probably could've just been a dice system, except that the tech tree demands some unit differentiation).

Almost every tech card (you just get a deck of tech cards per player) offers a special power, and acquiring "culture" cards usually gives you some (unthematic) take-that ability. Upon these realizations and also having finished discovering the board, my patience with the game was exhausted.

I don't think 4X is a promising genre for boardgames. Exploration is inherently too unpredictable, and it seems like much of the expanding and exploiting is meant to be played as MPS.

RATING: 7.5
Rating after two plays with two and four players. The game probably really is an "unplugged" version of the Civilization Revolution boardgame. On one hand, there are a lot of ways to get a Euro-style victory engine going, on the other there seem to plenty of ways (mainly through cardplay) to annoy your fellow players. Otherwise, player interaction is taking a backseat in this game. There is plenty to keep track of, but since the game itself is pretty straightforward, I found the rules easy to remember. All in all, it is one of the best first time experiences I had with an FFG game - about the same as I had with Arkham Horror and Chaos In The Old World. On my first play, I already liked it better than the well-received Runewars. For me, the only real drawback of this game is its long(!) playing time.

RATING: 7
12/2010:7.5
A loooong game. Even when we totally understood the game, the pace was slow. We were 4 hours into the game and still midway through. The game is Fantasy Flight fiddly but captures the overall CIV theme quite well. I enjoyed the exploration and city building part but am lukewarm about the battle mechanics. We had one souped up pikemen beat tanks with the use of a tech special event. Something which does not quite make sense. The culture track was a good addition to the game. 

Overall, the game is fun and engaging but the length of the game is prohibitive. Therefore, the ranking won't be going up the roof.

RATING: 5
I was quite dissapointed with the game. It had surprisingly many superficial similiraties to the computer game (which I like a lot) but I don't thimk these choices work optimally in a board game... 

The main problems I had with the game:
- the production/trade/culture collection mechanism (around your cities) was very boring. Calculating each time the same stuff felt just very unelegant and teadious.
- The combat was very abstract and not at all satisfying. 
- If you don't get to build your second city very early, you are basically out from the game.
- I believe that the higher level techs, units etc, don't have too much effect in the game. If one of the players is able to develop those techs, the game is so close to the end that players cannot really use these too much. The game doesn't really therefore span the whole history of the world.
- The wonders were very uninteresting and weak. As the early ones were too expensive to build, the more powerful ones didn't even come to play in our game.

My main problem for me in the game was however that the game didn't feel like proper epic civilization building game. It just didn't feel that I was building huge civilization, nor that each turn would take hundreds of years. Maybe it was because the high level of abstraction? The tech pyramid had some nice new idea (although many techs just felt superior) and was maybe the best part in the game IMHO.

RATING: 7.5
This game is a huge bargain.  You really get a lot for the money.

It is probably the best designed FFG game I have played, simply because randomness has a minimized role.  In my opinion, long games should never be decided by chance.

The instructions are actually a bit lacking.   There is an unofficial faq in the forums, but a number of common questions are still unanswered.  This isn't really a surprise, because there are so many different techs that there are bound to be some complex interactions between them.

My biggest complaint so far is that military seems to be unstoppable.  One player is bound to try for a different type of victory, and their capital will be smashed by someone that researches military.

RATING: 6
I wasn't this excited to play a game since the release of [gameid=20551], or maybe even ever.  Intially, the game met and even exceed my expectations.  Fantasy Flight managed to turn the Civilization franchise into something that suits its strengths perfectly.  Components are great, decisions are juicy, and the computer game is mimicked very well, both in terms of mechanics and in terms of feel.  The exploration is there, followed by the midgame conflict, and finally the endgame maximization for victory conditions.  

As we've gotten better, further plays have revealed a ruthless and irreversible snowball effect.  Good luck with the tiles, army draw, or barbarians will set up a runaway leader, and bad luck or a mistake is impossible to recover from.  All of our recent games have seen half of the players effectively eliminated within the first four turns.  The expansions don't offer any help here.  Both of them rebalance the victory conditions, but they make an early lead even more powerful, and the midgame devolves into a quick coronation of the early leader via a military steamroller, economic, or culture victory.

Alas, Civ, for what might have been.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The tech tree system is very well done, simple but easy to do, and can be quite varied from game to game.

RATING: 5
I knew what I was getting in to with this game, but I'm not sure how I feel about it at this point.  It's not the length (though that certainly doesn't hurt), but there's an awful lot that players need to know about the game when playing it to do well at it.  I like the way that the technologies affect what you can build, but to get your engine running efficiently, you have to know what all of the technologies do at the start of the game.  That's not a criticism, necessarily, but it does slow down the progress of the game with new players, and means that it takes a few games to really get how it all works.  This was the case with Through the Ages, but when I finished playing that game for the first time, I came away thinking that I had just played an extraordinary game.  After playing Civilization, I feel like this is just an OK game.  Maybe future playings will make the difference, though.

1 play: 3 players

RATING: N/A
Civilization: The Board Game is inspired by the legendary video game series created by Sid Meier. Players are tasked with guiding an entire civilization throughout the ages, taking ownership of your people’s technology, economy, culture, and military, as well as all the choices that go along with them. There are four different paths to victory, and each is riddled with opposition.
\13\\13\
In Civilization: The Board Game, 2-4 players take on the roles of famous leaders in charge of historical civilizations, each with their own abilities. Players will be able to explore a module game board, build cities and buildings, fight battles, research powerful technology, and attract great people by advancing their culture. No matter what your play style is, there is a civilization for you!
\13\\13\
Fans of Sid Meier’s classic video game franchise will find familiar footing in Civilization: The Board Game. Staying true to the foundations of its video game predecessor while creating a new and unique way to play, Civilization: The Board Game captures the spirit and grandeur of carving out a magnificent empire from modest beginnings.
\13\\13\
Players start off with a single city, one army figure, and one scout, and from these meager origins you must forge through the ages and become the greatest civilization in the world.

RATING: 7.5
I wanted to buy a boardgame version of the Videogame and i got exactly that. 
It delivers what its meant to do.

Pro: 
-It does what civ (expecially civ revolution) does. Its the videogame condensed in a boardgame

-huge variety and real 4x (can win with lot of different tactics)

-No dominant strategy and a strategic complexity far superior to most eurogames

-visually appealing

-lot of different civs with different tactics

Con:

-Long setup (not extremely long but still long)

-Duration (realistically 3 hours or little more)

-LOT of rules

-combat system is bad (thanks god its a 4x and its not mandatory).



If you are looking for a table version of the game, this is still the way to go.
If you are into old style eurogames, this might not being a good choice (too complex and even small interaction requires constant adaptation like the VG). 

expansions are highly recommended.

RATING: 4
I know [b][i]Civilization: The Board Game [/i][/b]will be a dream design for some people, but I'm not one of them. I've never played [i]Civ[/i] (or almost any other PC game) on the computer, so I came at this game cold and it felt like I had to slaughter a cow myself just to get the burger I wanted. So many details, so many bits, so much to remember – yet so little of interest given the weight of what's involved when playing.

In other fora I've expressed unbridled excitement over Carl Chudyk's [i]Innovation[/i], a card game with a civilization gloss on its wildly interactive and fun design. While lacking in thematic realism, [i]Innovation[/i] provides me with far more game play for far less work than this game.

Very tentative rating after one 3-player session. Interesting mix of heavy Euro elements - multiple resource tracking and purchase of tech upgrades. The latter in particular (and its interaction with the resource pools) felt a lot like Wallace's "Rise of Empires". Wargame elements such as there are (build up of units, and combat mini game) feel tacked on. Lots of little surprises in the game - build a steam boat, and you can land a capitol invasion to win a quick war victory - that reward rules mastery.

Good game, but if pressed for a deep 3-4 hour multiplayer session I'd rather play either a full on wargame or the aforementioned Rise of Empires.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
I really thought I might like this one... but my first experience was *really* bad.  (not really the games fault... I had trying to learn a game from someone reading the rules out loud for the very first time).

If you like Sid Meier's computer game this will certainly feel familiar... but I certainly spent our whole session wondering why I wasn't just playing on the computer.  That being said the special resource aquisition is neat, the combat seems potentially more strategic than it first appears from the "rock paper scissors" mechanic, and the tech tree is just great.  However, I will never be a fan of long games with high potential for player elimination... so I'm not sure how keen I will be to give this one another shot.

RATING: 8
A super-meaty civ-building game with four distinct independent win conditions and variable player powers. Win by conquering the other players (of COURSE this had to be one), cultural development, economic power, or technological advancement.

Combat is based on a simple diceless(yay!) card system which allows for simple but strong power upgrades.

My favorite 3-hourish game so far.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The boardgame repeats many features and nuances from the computer game series admirably. Yet there is very little bookkeeping or tedious calculation, which is even more admirable, considering the mechanics of the computer game. Simple rules but many strategies, thanks to all the special abilities of the different civilizations, technologies and such. Just like the computer game, Sid Meier's Civilization The Boardgame always makes you want to play just one more game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
